The Virtue of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq
The commentator extols Abu Bakr's virtue and cites a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Abu Bakr is considered the most virtuous Companion after the prophets, based on the hadith cited by the commentator.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr is the greatest of the Companions after the prophets.

In this passage, the commentator highlights the unparalleled status of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq, the elder of the Companions. He not only states his absolute superiority but also provides a prophetic hadith as evidence. The mention of his companionship with the Prophet in the cave further underscores his virtue.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text carefully, noting the structure: a statement of virtue followed by a hadith and explanatory glosses.
2
Identify the key claim
The commentator asserts that Abu Bakr is the most virtuous of the Companions absolutely. Find the phrase that states this.
The phrase is 'أفضلهم على الإطلاق'.
3
Examine the proof
The commentator quotes a hadith to support the claim. Note the wording of the hadith and its implications.
4
Understand the glosses
The commentator explains certain words like 'تنويهاً' and 'إذ هو'. These clarify the reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على الصحابة رضي الله عنهم تنويهاً بعظم شأنه، إذ هو شيخ الصَّحابة وأفضلهم على الإطلاق، وفي ذكر مرافقته في الغار إشارة إلى ذلك أيضاً . مكة، والغار: ثقب في أعلى جبل ثور على مسيرة نحو ساعة من دخله النَّبيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم هو وأبو بكر حين خرجا مهاجرين من مكة إلى قوله: (تنويهاً) : أي : إعلاماً. قوله : (إذ هو): تعليل لما قبله. قوله: (وأفضلهم على الإطلاق أي لما في الحديث: «ما طلعتِ الشَّمسُ ولا غربت على أحدٍ بعدَ النَّبيِّينَ والمرسلين أفضل من أبي بكر (۱). قوله : (إلى ذلك) : أي : إلى أفضليته . قوله : (والغار : ثقب ... إلخ) : أي : ويسمى بغار ثور. قوله: (حين خرجا من مكة ... إلخ) : أي: بإذن الله تعالى لنبيه في مكة... الهجرة، وذلك أنه خرج إلى عقبة منى في الموسم، وهو وقت اجتماع النَّاس كل سنة يعرض نفسه على قبائل العرب، فلقيه بعضهم عند العقبة فدعاهم إلى الإسلام، فأسلم منهم ستّة نفر . لقيه في العام القابل اثنا عشر رجلاً منهم، فأسلموا ثم رجعوا وأظهروا الإسلام في بلدهم، ثمَّ قدم في العام القابل نحو سبعين رجلاً، فبايعهم على أن يمنعوه بما يمنعون عن نسائهم وأبنائهم، وعلى حرب
Discourse on the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them: As a declaration (tanwīhan) of the greatness of his status, for he is the elder of the Companions and the most virtuous of them absolutely, and the mention of his companionship in the cave is an indication of that as well. Makkah. And the cave: a hole in the top of Mount Thawr, about an hour's distance away, which the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم and Abu Bakr entered when they set out as emigrants from Makkah to... His statement: '(tanwīhan)' means an announcement. His statement: '(idh huwa)' is a cause for what precedes. His statement: '(and the most virtuous of them absolutely)' means because of the hadith: 'The sun did not rise nor set upon anyone after the Prophets and Messengers better than Abu Bakr.' His statement: '(to that)' means to his superiority. His statement: '(and the cave: a hole... etc.)' means it is also called Ghar Thawr. His statement: '(when they left Makkah... etc.)' means with the permission of Allah Almighty to His Prophet in Makkah... the emigration, and that he went to the 'Aqaba of Mina in the season, which is the time when people gather every year, presenting himself to the Arab tribes. Some of them met him at al-'Aqaba and he invited them to Islam, and six men from them accepted Islam. Twelve men from them met him the following year, and they accepted Islam, then they returned and manifested Islam in their land. Then the following year about seventy men came, and he took a pledge from them that they would protect him as they protect their women and children, and to war.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Companions, specifically praising Abu Bakr al-Siddiq. The commentator explains that the phrase 'tanwīhan' means to make known his great status, and 'idh huwa' provides the reason: he is the elder and most virtuous of the Companions absolutely. The mention of his companionship in the cave (Ghar Thawr) is another indication of his virtue. The commentator then cites a hadith (attributed to Abu Nuʿaym and Ahmad) stating that the sun did not rise or set on anyone better than Abu Bakr after the prophets and messengers. Next, he describes the cave as a hole in Mount Thawr, an hour's walk from Makkah, where the Prophet and Abu Bakr hid during the hijrah. He then details the events leading to the hijrah: the Prophet went to the 'Aqaba of Mina during the pilgrimage season, presenting himself to the tribes. Six men from Yathrib (later Medina) accepted Islam at the first meeting. The next year, twelve men came and accepted Islam, then returned and spread Islam in their city. The following year, about seventy men came and pledged allegiance to the Prophet (the Second Pledge of al-ʿAqaba) to protect him as they protect their women and children, and to fight alongside him. The passage concludes with the ongoing war context.

Key Terms
الصحابة
أبو بكر
الصديق
الحديث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Abu Bakr's virtue is based solely on the cave incident.
Correction: The commentator also cites a hadith explicitly stating his superiority over all others after the prophets.

Checkpoint
What evidence does the commentator provide for Abu Bakr being the most virtuous of the Companions?
A) His wealth and lineage
B) The hadith that the sun did not rise or set on anyone better after the prophets
C) His age and experience
D) His knowledge of the Quran

Key Takeaway
Abu Bakr's virtue is established by a prophetic hadith that declares him the best of creation after the prophets.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet ﷺ declared Abu Bakr the best after him. Do you truly honor Abu Bakr in your heart as the second greatest human ever? What action today can prove that?
?
You say you love the Companions, but do you prioritize following the example of Abu Bakr, the most virtuous of them? Name one specific decision you will make this week based on his character.
?
The sun rises and sets on millions of people, yet only one after the prophets is declared the best. If you were to be asked where you stand in loving and obeying Allah, what would your answer be? What will you change tonight?

The Hijrah and the Cave of Thawr
The commentator describes the cave and the Aqaba pledges preceding the hijrah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the events of the hijrah: the Prophet’s concealment in the cave and the pivotal pledges at Aqaba.

Core Idea
The hijrah was a divinely orchestrated journey, aided by the cave and the pledges of support from the Ansar.

This passage vividly narrates the Prophet’s journey from Makkah to Medina, beginning with the cave on Mount Thawr where he and Abu Bakr hid. It then traces the pre-hijrah encounters with the people of Yathrib at al-Aqaba, culminating in the second pledge of allegiance that secured protection for the Prophet and his followers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage noting the sequence of events: the cave description, then the meetings at Aqaba.
2
Visualize the cave
The cave is described as a hole in Mount Thawr, an hour from Makkah. Consider its significance for the hijrah.
3
Trace the Aqaba meetings
The passage mentions three successive years: first six men, then twelve, then about seventy. Note the progression.
4
Understand the pledge
The final pledge included a commitment to protect the Prophet and to war. This was the foundation of the Islamic state in Medina.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على الصحابة رضي الله عنهم تنويهاً بعظم شأنه، إذ هو شيخ الصَّحابة وأفضلهم على الإطلاق، وفي ذكر مرافقته في الغار إشارة إلى ذلك أيضاً . مكة، والغار: ثقب في أعلى جبل ثور على مسيرة نحو ساعة من دخله النَّبيُّ صلى الله عليه وسلم هو وأبو بكر حين خرجا مهاجرين من مكة إلى قوله: (تنويهاً) : أي : إعلاماً. قوله : (إذ هو): تعليل لما قبله. قوله: (وأفضلهم على الإطلاق أي لما في الحديث: «ما طلعتِ الشَّمسُ ولا غربت على أحدٍ بعدَ النَّبيِّينَ والمرسلين أفضل من أبي بكر (۱). قوله : (إلى ذلك) : أي : إلى أفضليته . قوله : (والغار : ثقب ... إلخ) : أي : ويسمى بغار ثور. قوله: (حين خرجا من مكة ... إلخ) : أي: بإذن الله تعالى لنبيه في مكة... الهجرة، وذلك أنه خرج إلى عقبة منى في الموسم، وهو وقت اجتماع النَّاس كل سنة يعرض نفسه على قبائل العرب، فلقيه بعضهم عند العقبة فدعاهم إلى الإسلام، فأسلم منهم ستّة نفر . لقيه في العام القابل اثنا عشر رجلاً منهم، فأسلموا ثم رجعوا وأظهروا الإسلام في بلدهم، ثمَّ قدم في العام القابل نحو سبعين رجلاً، فبايعهم على أن يمنعوه بما يمنعون عن نسائهم وأبنائهم، وعلى حرب
Discourse on the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them: As a declaration (tanwīhan) of the greatness of his status, for he is the elder of the Companions and the most virtuous of them absolutely, and the mention of his companionship in the cave is an indication of that as well. Makkah. And the cave: a hole in the top of Mount Thawr, about an hour's distance away, which the Prophet صلى الله عليه وسلم and Abu Bakr entered when they set out as emigrants from Makkah to... His statement: '(tanwīhan)' means an announcement. His statement: '(idh huwa)' is a cause for what precedes. His statement: '(and the most virtuous of them absolutely)' means because of the hadith: 'The sun did not rise nor set upon anyone after the Prophets and Messengers better than Abu Bakr.' His statement: '(to that)' means to his superiority. His statement: '(and the cave: a hole... etc.)' means it is also called Ghar Thawr. His statement: '(when they left Makkah... etc.)' means with the permission of Allah Almighty to His Prophet in Makkah... the emigration, and that he went to the 'Aqaba of Mina in the season, which is the time when people gather every year, presenting himself to the Arab tribes. Some of them met him at al-'Aqaba and he invited them to Islam, and six men from them accepted Islam. Twelve men from them met him the following year, and they accepted Islam, then they returned and manifested Islam in their land. Then the following year about seventy men came, and he took a pledge from them that they would protect him as they protect their women and children, and to war.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the Companions, specifically praising Abu Bakr al-Siddiq. The commentator explains that the phrase 'tanwīhan' means to make known his great status, and 'idh huwa' provides the reason: he is the elder and most virtuous of the Companions absolutely. The mention of his companionship in the cave (Ghar Thawr) is another indication of his virtue. The commentator then cites a hadith (attributed to Abu Nuʿaym and Ahmad) stating that the sun did not rise or set on anyone better than Abu Bakr after the prophets and messengers. Next, he describes the cave as a hole in Mount Thawr, an hour's walk from Makkah, where the Prophet and Abu Bakr hid during the hijrah. He then details the events leading to the hijrah: the Prophet went to the 'Aqaba of Mina during the pilgrimage season, presenting himself to the tribes. Six men from Yathrib (later Medina) accepted Islam at the first meeting. The next year, twelve men came and accepted Islam, then returned and spread Islam in their city. The following year, about seventy men came and pledged allegiance to the Prophet (the Second Pledge of al-ʿAqaba) to protect him as they protect their women and children, and to fight alongside him. The passage concludes with the ongoing war context.

Checkpoint

A) Six
B) Twelve
C) Seventy
D) Forty

Key Takeaway
The hijrah was facilitated by the hospitality and protection pledged by the Ansar at Aqaba, after the Prophet hid in the cave of Thawr.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Ansar pledged to protect the Prophet as they protect their own women and children. Think of the people Allah has placed under your care—your family, your community. Do you protect their faith and well-being with the same fierceness? What is one concrete way you can renew that commitment today?
?
The Prophet presented himself to the tribes year after year, facing rejection but persisting. Is there a truth you know—about Allah, about yourself—that you avoid sharing because you fear rejection? Will you speak it this week, trusting in the same Lord who prepared the hearts of the Ansar?
?
The hijrah meant leaving everything familiar. Is there a habit, a comfort, or a relationship you know you must leave behind to draw closer to Allah? What is stopping you from making that 'hijrah' today, even if it's just a small step?

The Virtue of Abu Bakr and Reason for Mentioning Him in the Cave
Commentator explains purpose of cave mention and affirms Abu Bakr's superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author mentions Abu Bakr's companionship in the cave and how it affirms his superior virtue among the Companions.

Core Idea
The cave mention is deliberate to elevate Abu Bakr's status, not just a historical detail.

This short passage explains the wisdom behind mentioning Abu Bakr's companionship in the cave. The commentator clarifies that the mention is not incidental but intended to elevate Abu Bakr's rank, as he is the foremost of the Companions. The hadith quoted reinforces his absolute superiority after the prophets.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the commentator is explaining key phrases from the main text.
Pay attention to the word 'تنويهاً' – it sets the purpose.
2
Identify the purpose of the cave mention
The passage states that mentioning the cave is 'تنويهاً بعظم شأنه' – to elevate his status. Understand that this is the main reason.
The commentator explicitly says 'إشارة إلى ذلك' – an indication of his superiority.
3
Examine the supporting evidence
The commentator cites a hadith that Abu Bakr is the best after prophets and messengers. This proves his absolute superiority.
The hadith is from authentic sources, establishing the doctrine.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الكلام على الصحابة رضي الله عنهم تنويهاً بعظم شأنه، إذ هو شيخ الصَّحابة وأفضلهم على الإطلاق، وفي ذكر مرافقته في الغار إشارة إلى ذلك أيضاً .
قوله: (تنويهاً) : أي : إعلاماً.
قوله : (إذ هو): تعليل لما قبله.
قوله: (وأفضلهم على الإطلاق أي لما في الحديث: «ما طلعتِ الشَّمسُ ولا غربت على أحدٍ بعدَ النَّبيِّينَ والمرسلين أفضل من أبي بكر (۱).
قوله : (إلى ذلك) : أي : إلى أفضليته .
Speech about the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, as an elevation of his status, for he is the elder of the Companions and the most virtuous of them absolutely, and in mentioning his companionship in the cave there is also an indication of that. His saying: (elevation) meaning: informing. His saying: (for he is) is a cause for what preceded. His saying: (and the most virtuous of them absolutely) because of the hadith: 'The sun has not risen nor set upon anyone after the prophets and messengers better than Abu Bakr (1).' His saying: (to that) meaning: to his superiority.
Source pages p. 100

Commentary
This passage is a commentary explaining why the author mentioned Abu Bakr's companionship in the cave. It states that this mention is to elevate his status, as he is the elder and most virtuous of the Companions absolutely. The commentator then explains key phrases: 'تنويهاً' means informing, 'إذ هو' provides the reason, and the hadith is cited to prove his absolute superiority. The phrase 'إلى ذلك' refers back to his superiority.

Key Terms

تنويهاً
أفضلهم على الإطلاق
الحديث

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the cave mention is only a historical detail with no theological significance.
Correction: The commentator shows it is deliberately mentioned to elevate Abu Bakr's status and affirm his superiority.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Mentioning Abu Bakr's cave companionship is a deliberate teaching point to affirm his supreme virtue among the Companions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe Abu Bakr (RA) is the best of this ummah after the prophets? If so, how does that belief shape your love and respect for him? If not, what is holding you back from accepting this explicit hadith?
?
This lesson shows that even a small detail like the cave mention is used to elevate Abu Bakr. Are you careful to recognize and honor the virtues of the Companions in your own life, or do you take them for granted?
?
The hadith says no one after prophets is better than Abu Bakr. Does your heart feel the weight of that statement? What would it take for you to make that belief a living reality in your devotion to the Sunnah?

The Meaning of 'al-Kharida al-Bahiyya'
Al-Sawi explains the meaning of the book's title and its components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the etymological and metaphorical significance of each word in the book's title 'al-Kharida al-Bahiyya'.

Core Idea
The title is a metaphor: a luminous unperforated pearl symbolizing the book's clarity and preciousness.

Al-Sawi begins by unpacking the book's title, showing that every word is carefully chosen to convey meaning and attract the reader. He explains that 'al-Kharida' means an unperforated pearl, 'al-Bahiyya' means luminous, and together they form a metaphor for the book's clarity and value. This passage also demonstrates the author's method of using language and Quranic references to support his explanations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the full literal translation. Notice the flow of Al-Sawi's explanation.
The passage includes Quranic citations; note how they support the meanings.
2
Identify the explanation of each word
Find where Al-Sawi explains 'Saniyya', 'al-Kharida', and 'al-Bahiyya'. Note the original meanings and derivations.
'Saniyya' comes from 'al-sana' (light); 'al-Kharida' is an unperforated pearl; 'al-Bahiyya' from 'al-baha' (light/beauty).
3
Understand the metaphor
Al-Sawi states that the author borrowed the name (istihara) to match the name with the named. Identify the thing being likened (the book) and the thing it is likened to (a luminous pearl).
The common factor is preciousness (nafasa).
4
Reflect on the purpose
Consider why the author chose such a title. What does it tell you about the book's content and style?
The title aims to evoke interest and convey the book's value and clarity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى الخريدة (سنيَّة) نسبة إلى السَّنا - بالقصر - وهو النُّور؛ يعني: أنَّها واضحة الدلالة على معانيها . (سميتُها الخريدة البهية الجملة صفة (عقيدة)، والخريدة في الأصل: اللؤلؤة التي لم تثقب، و(البهيَّة) نعت لـ«الخريدة»، و(البها): الضّياء، واستعار لها هذا الاسم ليطابق الاسم المسمى، ثم ذكر من ثمَّ نعوتها أيضاً ما يقتضي الرغبة في تناولها فقال: الكل مجازاً مرسلاً، والعلاقة الجزئية.

قوله: (وهو النُّور): أي: ويعبر عنه بالضّياء، قال تعالى: يَكَادُ سَنَا بَرْقِهِ يَذْهَبُ بِالْأَبْصَرِ)

قوله: (الجملة صفة عقيدة) : أي : جملة سميتها ... إلخ، وهو نعت بالجملة بعد النعت بالمفرد فإنَّ سنيّة نعت أوّل وهو مفرد نظير قوله تعالى: (قَدْ جَاءَكُم مِّنَ اللَّهِ نُورٌ وَكِتَبٌ مُّبِينٌ يَهْدِى بِهِ... إلخ.

قوله : ( والبها: الضّياء) أي : ويطلق على الحُسن والجمال، وهو بالمقام وإن كان الأوّل مناسباً أيضاً.

قوله: (واستعار لها هذا الاسم) : أي : فقد شبه كتابه هذا باللُّؤلؤة مضيئة لم تثقب، بجامع النفاسة في كل، واستعار اللفظ الدال على المشبَّه
The meaning of al-Kharida: (Saniyya) is a relative adjective from al-sana (with shortening) which is light; meaning that it is clear in its indication of its meanings. (I named it al-Kharida al-Bahiyya) — the sentence is an attribute of ('aqida), and al-kharida originally is the pearl that has not been pierced, and (al-Bahiyya) is an attribute of 'al-Kharida', and (al-baha') is light/radiance, and he borrowed this name for it to match the name with the named. Then he mentioned after that also its attributes that necessitate desire for its acquisition, saying: all of it is metonymical (majaz mursal) with a part-whole relationship.

His saying: (and it is light): that is, and it is expressed as al-diya', Almighty Allah said: 'The lightning's flash almost takes away the eyesight' (Q. 24:43).

His saying: (the sentence is an attribute of 'aqida): that is, the sentence 'I named it ...' etc., and it is an attributive sentence after the single-word attribute, for Saniyya is the first attribute and it is a single word, similar to Almighty Allah's saying: 'Indeed there has come to you from Allah a light and a clear Book ...' (Q. 5:15-16).

His saying: (and al-baha' is al-diya'): that is, and it is also used for beauty and loveliness, and it is in the context, although the first meaning is also suitable.

His saying: (and he borrowed this name for it): that is, he likened this book of his to a luminous pearl that has not been pierced, with the common factor of preciousness in each, and he borrowed the word indicating the thing likened to (mushabbah bihi).
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage explains the etymology and meaning of the book's title 'الخريدة البهية'. The commentator begins by analyzing the word 'سنيَّة', stating it is derived from 'السَّنا' (light), indicating the book's clear indication of its meanings. He then explains that the sentence 'سميتها الخريدة البهية' is an attribute of 'عقيدة'. 'الخريدة' in its original meaning is an unperforated pearl, and 'البهيَّة' is an attribute meaning luminous. The commentator notes that the author borrowed this name to match the name with the named. Then he mentions that the author further described the book with attributes that encourage its study, and that 'الكل' is a metonymy (مجاز مرسل) with a part-whole relationship. The commentator then provides additional glosses: for 'وهو النُّور', it means 'الضّياء', and quotes Quran 24:43. For 'الجملة صفة عقيدة', he clarifies that the sentence is an attributive sentence after a single-word attribute, similar to Quran 5:15-16. For 'والبها: الضياء', he notes it can also mean beauty, and here both are suitable. Finally, he explains the metaphor in 'واستعار لها هذا الاسم': the author likened his book to a luminous unperforated pearl due to its preciousness.

Key Terms
الخريدة
البهيَّة
السَّنا
البها
استعارة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That 'al-Kharida' simply means 'pearl' without the nuance of being unperforated.
Correction: The text explicitly states 'al-Kharida fi al-asl: al-lu'lu'a allati lam tuthqab' (the pearl that has not been pierced), indicating its pristine and untouched nature.

Checkpoint
What is the original meaning of the word 'الخريدة'?
A) A shining star
B) An unperforated pearl
C) A beautiful garden
D) A precious stone

Key Takeaway
The title 'al-Kharida al-Bahiyya' uses a pearl metaphor to convey the book's clarity, purity, and preciousness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author compared his book to a luminous unperforated pearl. When you study this book, do you treat each phrase with that same sense of preciousness, or do you let your mind skim over it? What would change if you read every sentence as if it were part of a priceless pearl?
?
Al-Sawi says the title is clear in its indication. How clearly do you seek to understand what you read? Are you content with surface meanings, or do you dig for the light beneath, as the author intended?
?
The metaphor suggests the book is both pure (unperforated) and radiant (luminous). Which of these two qualities is missing from your current approach to this text? What one step can you take today to recapture that quality?

Core Idea
Nasir al-Din dedicated his life to teaching and preserving the Prophet's hadith.

This passage introduces us to Nasir al-Din, a prominent scholar of hadith. We learn about his position, his activities, and his written works. This brief biography helps us appreciate the scholarly tradition in which he lived and the contributions he made.

The Prophet’s Parents: Believers of the True Religion
Al-Sawi defends the faith of the Prophet's parents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Prophet’s parents were monotheists upon the religion of Ibrahim and therefore believers destined for Paradise.

Core Idea
The Prophet’s light descended through pure loins and pure wombs.

Al-Sawi clarifies a crucial point about the lineage of the Prophet: his parents and ancestors were not pagans but followers of the pure monotheism of Ibrahim. This passage defends their status as believers, refuting common misconceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow. Notice the linguistic note about breaking the 'dal' and the theological claim about the Prophet's parents.
The passage includes a footnote referenced as (1).
2
Understand the linguistic point
The phrase 'upon his parents' (والديه) is best read with a broken 'dal' (walidayhi) to include forefathers. This shows that the discussion extends to ancestors.
This grammatical nuance prepares for the next point.
3
Grasp the theological claim
Al-Sawi affirms that the Prophet's parents were upon the religion of Ibrahim, making them believers of Paradise. He rejects contrary opinions and cites the transmission of the Prophet's light from pure loins to pure wombs as evidence.
The 'light transmission' is a known concept in Islamic tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وعلى والديه الأحسن كسر الدال ليشمل الأجداد (۱) . قوله إلى الإنس والجنّ) : أي : إرسال تكليف. وقوله : (بل للخلق جميعاً) (۲) : أي : ولكن إرساله للجمادات والحيوانات الغير العاقلة إرسال تشريف وللملائكة قيل : تكليف، وقيل: تشريف، وللثّقلين إرسال تكليف. (1) أي: لأنهم كانوا على ملة إبراهيم، فهم مؤمنون من أهل الجنة، ولا عبرة بقول من قال في أبويه خلاف ذلك بل الذي اشتهر أن نوره عليه الصلاة والسلام كان ينتقل من أصلاب الفاخرة إلى الأرحام الطاهرة، فهذا يدل على أن أبويه وأجداده كانوا على دين حق؛ أي على ملة إبراهيم. «تقريرات بصيلة». (۲) فبعثته مع العاملة في الزمان والمكان إلى جميع المكلفين، فرسالته عامة إلى يوم القيامة، وكذا شريعته باقية إليها .
His statement: 'And upon his parents' – the best is to break the dal to include the ancestors (1). His statement: 'to humans and jinn' – meaning: a mission of obligation (taklif). And his statement: 'rather to all creation' (2) – meaning: but his mission to inanimate objects and non-rational animals is a mission of honor (tashrif), and to angels, it is said: obligation, and it is said: honor, and to the two weighty ones (humans and jinn) it is a mission of obligation. (1) Meaning: because they (his parents) were upon the religion of Ibrahim, so they are believers from the people of Paradise, and no attention is paid to the saying of those who say otherwise about his parents. Rather, what is famous is that his light (peace be upon him) was transmitted from pure loins to pure wombs, and this indicates that his parents and ancestors were upon the true religion, i.e., the religion of Ibrahim. 'Taqrirat Basila.' (2) So his mission is with the condition of time and place to all those who are obligated, so his message is general until the Day of Resurrection, and likewise his law remains until it.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two phrases from the previous lesson: 'upon his parents' and 'to all creation'. The first note explains that the phrase 'upon his parents' is best understood with a broken 'dal' (i.e., 'walidayhi' to include forefathers). The footnote then clarifies that the Prophet's parents were followers of the religion of Ibrahim (monotheists) and therefore are believers destined for Paradise. It rejects claims to the contrary and asserts that the transmission of the Prophet's light through pure loins and wombs proves their true faith. The second note explains the scope of the Prophet's mission: to humans and jinn it is a mission of religious obligation; to inanimate objects and animals it is a mission of honor; to angels it is either obligation or honor; and his message and law are universal and eternal until the Day of Judgment.

Key Terms
والديه
ملة إبراهيم
نوره عليه الصلاة والسلام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet's parents were disbelievers (polytheists).
Correction: Al-Sawi affirms they were upon the religion of Ibrahim, which is pure monotheism, and thus they are believers from the people of Paradise.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the basis for considering the Prophet's parents as believers?
A) They were followers of the religion of Ibrahim
B) They were from the Quraysh tribe
C) They had a vision predicting prophethood
D) They performed miracles

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's parents and ancestors were monotheists upon the religion of Ibrahim, as evidenced by the pure transmission of his light.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the knowledge that the Prophet's ancestors were monotheists upon the religion of Ibrahim move you to consider the legacy of faith you are leaving for your own children? Will you strive to ensure that your lineage remains upon tawhid until the Day of Judgment?
?
The Prophet's light was passed through pure lineages. Reflect on your own spiritual lineage: are you a pure link in that chain? What are you doing right now to purify your heart and actions so that the light of faith continues through you?
?
Al-Sawi dismissed baseless claims about the Prophet's parents. Are there any doubts about the Prophet's honor and purity that you have entertained? Today, commit to strengthening your conviction in the noble status of the Prophet's family, starting with his blessed parents.

The Universal Mission of the Prophet
Al-Sawi explains the scope of the Prophet's mission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the different categories of the Prophet's mission: obligation to humans and jinn, honor to non-rational beings, and the eternal nature of his message.

Core Idea
The Prophet's mission is universal and eternal, covering all creation.

Al-Sawi explains that the Prophet's mission is not limited to humans and jinn but extends to all creation, though in different modes. He also emphasizes that his message and law remain until the Day of Resurrection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the distinction between 'إرسال تكليف' (mission of obligation) and 'إرسال تشريف' (mission of honor).
The passage includes footnote (2).
2
Identify the three categories
The passage lists: 1) humans and jinn (obligation), 2) inanimate objects and non-rational animals (honor), 3) angels (disputed: obligation or honor). Note the difference.
The term 'الثقلين' refers to humans and jinn.
3
Grasp the universal and eternal scope
Al-Sawi concludes that the Prophet's mission is general to all responsible beings in time and place, and his law remains until the Day of Resurrection. This establishes the finality and universality of his message.
This supports the Islamic belief in the Prophet's seal of prophethood.

?

Biography of al-Nafrawi
Editor provides a brief biography of al-Nafrawi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key biographical details of the Maliki scholar al-Nafrawi and his importance in Islamic scholarship.

Core Idea
Al-Nafrawi was a renowned Maliki scholar whose commentary on the Risala remains a key reference.

This short passage introduces the scholar al-Nafrawi, whose work 'al-Fawakih al-Dawani' is a major commentary on a foundational Maliki text. Knowing the author's background helps us appreciate the authority behind the commentary. Let's examine the details given.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the biographical information provided.
2
Note the full name and titles
Observe the full name: Shihab al-Din Abu al-Abbas Ahmad ibn Ghunaym ibn Salim al-Nafrawi al-Azhari al-Maliki. These titles indicate his affiliation with al-Azhar and the Maliki school.
The honorific 'Shihab al-Din' means 'flame of the religion'.

(١) هو الإمام العلامة شهاب الدين أبو العباس أحمد بن غنيم بن سالم النفراوي الأزهري المالكي كان من أفراد العالم علماً وفضلاً وذكاء، توفي الله سنة (١١٢٦هـ) في القاهرة، من مؤلفاته: «الفواكه الدواني شرح رسالة أبي زيد القيرواني». ينظر سلك الدرر (١٤۸/١) ، وشجرة النور الزكية) (٤٦٠/١ ) .

Among his works: 'al-Fawakih al-Dawani, a commentary on the Risala of Abu Zayd al-Qayrawani.' See Sulk al-Durar (1/148) and Shajarat al-Nur al-Zakiyya (1/460).

The Highest Rank of Siddiqiyya: Abu Bakr's Unique Excellence
Al-Sawi defends the superiority of Abu Bakr's Siddiqiyya rank with hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Al-Sawi affirms that Abu Bakr holds the highest rank of Siddiqiyya after the prophets, supported by prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
Abu Bakr's rank of Siddiqiyya is the highest after prophethood — none can equal it.

Al-Sawi explains a line from the main text that states the degrees of Siddiqiyya are varied, with Abu Bakr's rank being the most elevated. He provides two hadiths to substantiate this claim, emphasizing that no other person in the Muslim community has reached that level. This lesson examines that assertion and the evidence behind it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice how Al-Sawi directly comments on the main text.
The Arabic ends with a truncated word; the translation completes it as 'would outweigh.'
2
Understand Al-Sawi's claim
Al-Sawi states that the highest rank of Siddiqiyya is Abu Bakr's, and no other Muslim—past or present—has attained it, let alone those from other nations.
This is a strong statement of superiority.
3
Examine the hadith evidence
Two hadiths are cited: first, the Prophet (peace be upon him) said Abu Bakr is the best after the prophets; second, his faith outweighs that of the entire nation.
These hadiths are used as proof for the claim.
4
Reflect on the significance
Consider why this rank is unique. The hadiths show Abu Bakr's unparalleled excellence among the Companions and all believers.
This reinforces the special status of Abu Bakr in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وأعلاها رتبة أبي بكر الصِّدِّيق :أي: ولم يرتق إليها غيره من باقي الأمَّة المحمَّديَّة، فضلاً عن سائر الأمم ؛ لما في الحديث الشريف: ما طلعت الشَّمسُ على أحدٍ بعد النَّبيِّين أفضل من أبي بكر، وفي رواية أيضاً: «لو وزن إيمان أبي بكرٍ بإيمان الأمَّة «الرجح
He said: (And the highest of them is the rank of Abu Bakr al-Siddiq): meaning, none other from the rest of the Muhammadan community has attained it, let alone other nations; due to the noble hadith: 'The sun has not risen upon anyone after the prophets better than Abu Bakr,' and in another narration: 'If the faith of Abu Bakr were weighed against the faith of the nation, it would outweigh.'
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase from the main text that states the ranks of Siddiqiyya are varied, and the highest is that of Abu Bakr. Al-Sawi explains that no other person in the Muslim community has reached that rank, and certainly no one from previous communities. He supports this with two hadiths: one stating that Abu Bakr is the best after the prophets, and another that his faith outweighs that of the entire nation.

Key Terms
صديقية
أبو بكر الصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might believe that other companions are equal to Abu Bakr in rank.
Correction: The text emphasizes that Abu Bakr's rank is the highest and none other has attained it, supported by hadith that state he is best after prophets and his faith outweighs the nation.

Checkpoint

A) It is equal to the faith of all companions
B) It outweighs the faith of the entire nation
C) It is less than the faith of other prophets
D) It is not mentioned

Key Takeaway
Abu Bakr holds the unparalleled highest rank of Siddiqiyya, confirmed by prophetic hadith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

Lineage of Imam Malik
Full lineage of Imam Malik with spelling clarifications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the noble lineage of Imam Malik ibn Anas as recorded in traditional biographical sources.

Core Idea
Imam Malik's lineage traces back through a chain of honorable ancestors.

This brief passage from al-Sawi's commentary provides the full patrilineal lineage of Imam Malik ibn Anas. While it may appear to be a simple genealogy, in traditional Islamic scholarship, tracing an imam's lineage is a way of establishing his honorable descent and connection to the early Muslim community. Let's examine the text and its details.

Guided Path
1

Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation to get an overall sense of the lineage.

The comments clarify the correct spelling and pronunciation.

Reflect on why a theological commentary includes such genealogical details. It establishes the imam's noble birth and connection to Arab tribes.

Examine the footnote
The footnote cites a line of poetry attributed to 'Adi ibn Zayd. Consider its relevance, possibly as a verification or cultural context.
This may be a marginal note from the original text.

أمَّا مالك : فهو ابن أنس بن مالك بن أبي عامر بن عمرو بن حارث بن غميان - بمعجمة فمثناة تحتيّة - ابن خثيل - بخاء معجمة مضمومة، فمثلثة (۱) البيت لعدي بن زيد أخرجه الدينوري في المجالسة وجواهر العلم» (٥٢٣).

Commentary
This passage is a brief biographical note on Imam Malik, giving his lineage up to his great-great-grandfathers. It includes orthographic notes: 'Ghimyan' is spelled with a point (i.e., letter غ) and a ya' under (i.e., ي), and 'Khuthayl' is spelled with a خ with dammah and then a ث. The footnote at the end cites the source of a line of poetry, though it is likely a reference to the poetry mentioned earlier in the commentary. The focus here is on establishing Imam Malik's noble ancestry.

The Noble Biography of Imam Malik ibn Anas
Al-Sawi gives a biographical account of Imam Malik's lineage, birth, and status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the noble lineage, miraculous birth, and scholarly status of Imam Malik ibn Anas as recorded by traditional sources.

Core Idea
Imam Malik's extraordinary birth and noble lineage foreshadow his rise as a great imam.

In this passage, al-Sawi introduces Imam Malik as one of the four great imams and provides a detailed biographical sketch. You will learn about his distinguished ancestry, the remarkable length of his pregnancy, and his place among the early generations of Muslims. This background helps us appreciate the stature of the man behind the Maliki school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Imams
Read the opening sentence where al-Sawi explains the phrase 'except the schools of the four imams'. Note the names: Malik, al-Shafi'i, Abu Hanifa, and Ahmad ibn Hanbal.
This sets the context for the biography.
2
Trace the Lineage
Pay attention to the detailed lineage of Imam Malik, including the pronunciation of 'al-Asbahi' and his status as a client of covenant (mawla 'ahd) rather than a freed slave.
The text emphasizes his noble Arab and royal connections.
3
Reflect on the Birth Details
Consider the report that his mother carried him for three years or more, and the explanation that this length indicates great intellect. Also note the different reports about his birth and death years.
This sign is seen as a precursor to his scholarly achievements.
4
Examine His Family and Generation
Look at the religious standing of his father, grandfather, and great-grandfather. Then consider the discussion about whether he was a tabi'i or a follower of the tabi'in.
This shows the scholarly pedigree and the careful classification of generations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (سوی مذاهب الأئمة الأربعة: أي: وهم الإمام مالك والشَّافعي، وأبو حنيفة، وأحمد بن حنبل الله . أمَّا مالك : فهو ابن أنس بن مالك بن أبي عامر بن عمرو بن حارث بن غميان - بمعجمة فمثناة تحتيّة - ابن خثيل - بخاء معجمة مضمومة، فمثلة - الأصبحي، بفتح الباء ؛ نسبة إلى ذي أصبح، بطن من حمير وهو من العرب، عهده في قريش في بني تيم الله، فهو مولى عهد لا مولى عتاقة عند الجمهور، فهو من بيوت الملوك؛ لأنَّ القاعدة عند العرب إذا جاؤوا في النَّسب بذي يكون من النَّسب بذي يكون من ذلك، حملت به أمه ثلاث سنين، وقيل: أكثر ، وطول الحمل علامة على وفور عقل المولود. ولد سنة ثلاث وتسعين من الهجرة على الأشهر بذي المروءة، موضع من مساجد تبوك على ثمانية بُرد من المدينة ولا ينافيه قول عياض: إنّه الدار والمولد والمنشأ؛ لأنَّ ذا المروءة من أعمال المدينة، وقيل: ولد سنة تسعين ومات سنة تسع وسبعين ومئة، ودفن بالبقيع وقبره مدني مشهور . وكان أنس أبوه فقيهاً، وجده مالك كان من كبار التابعين، أحد الأربعة الذين حملوا عثمان إلى قبره ليلاً وغسلوه ودفنوه. وجده أبو عامر صحابي حضر مع المصطفى مغازيه كلَّها إلا بدراً. ومالك من أتباع التّابعين على الصَّحيح ، وقيل : من التابعين؛ لإدراكه ينظر ترتيب المدارك» (١٢٤/١).
His statement: 'Except the schools of the four imams', meaning: they are Imam Mālik, al-Shāfiʿī, Abū Ḥanīfa, and Aḥmad ibn Ḥanbal—may Allah be pleased with them. As for Mālik, he is the son of Anas ibn Mālik ibn Abī ʿĀmir ibn ʿAmr ibn Ḥārith ibn Ghamyān—with a shīn and a dhāl [؟]— ibn Khuthayl—with a khāʾ and a dhāl [؟]— al-Aṣbaḥī, with a fatḥa on the bāʾ; an attribution to Dhū Aṣbaḥ, a branch of Ḥimyar, and he is an Arab. His alliance is to Quraysh in the Banī Taym Allāh, so he is a client of covenant (mawlā ʿahd) not a client of emancipation (mawlā ʿitāqa) according to the majority, and he is from royal houses because the custom of the Arabs when they come with 'Dhū' in lineage, then... His mother carried him for three years, and it was said more, and the length of pregnancy is a sign of the abundance of the child's intellect. He was born in the year ninety-three of the Hijra according to the most famous report, in Dhī al-Murūʿa, a location among the mosques of Tabūk, eight burd from Medina, and this does not contradict the statement of ʿIyāḍ that it is the home, birthplace, and upbringing because Dhī al-Murūʿa is among the districts of Medina. And it was said he was born in the year ninety and died in the year one hundred and seventy-nine, and he was buried in al-Baqīʿ, and his grave is well-known in Medina. His father Anas was a jurist, and his grandfather Mālik was among the great tābiʿūn, one of the four who carried ʿUthmān to his grave at night, washed him, and buried him. And his grandfather Abū ʿĀmir was a Companion who attended all the battles of the Muṣṭafā except Badr. And Mālik, on the correct view, is from the followers of the tābiʿūn (atbāʿ al-tābiʿīn), and it was said he is from the tābiʿūn because he perceived [some Companions?]. See 'Tartīb al-Madārik' (1/124).
Source pages p. 441

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account. The author begins by explaining the phrase 'except the schools of the four imams' from the previous lesson, explicitly naming the four: Mālik, al-Shāfiʿī, Abū Ḥanīfa, and Aḥmad ibn Ḥanbal. He then provides a detailed biography of Imam Mālik. He traces his lineage back through several generations, noting the correct pronunciation of his nisba 'al-Aṣbaḥī' (with a fatḥa on the bā'), which derives from Dhū Aṣbaḥ, a branch of Ḥimyar. He clarifies that Mālik was a client of covenant (mawlā ʿahd) with the Banī Taym Allāh of Quraysh, not a freed slave, and thus of royal descent. He recounts an extraordinary detail: Mālik's mother was pregnant with him for three years (or more), which is taken as a sign of his great intellect. He gives the most famous birth year as 93 AH in Dhī al-Murūʿa (near Tabūk), reconciling it with a statement by ʿIyāḍ that it was in Medina by noting that the place is within Medina's administrative area. He gives another report of birth in 90 AH and death in 179 AH, burial in al-Baqīʿ. He notes the scholarly status of his father Anas (a jurist) and his grandfather Mālik (a great tābiʿī who helped bury ʿUthmān), and his great-grandfather Abū ʿĀmir (a Companion who attended all battles except Badr). Finally, he states that Imam Mālik is, on the correct view, from the generation after the tābiʿūn (atbāʿ al-tābiʿīn), though some said he was a tābiʿī because he encountered [Companions].

ذي أصبح

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The text explains he was a client of covenant (mawlā ʿahd) from noble royal lineage, not a freed slave.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Life and Lineage of Imam Malik
Al-Sawi presents biographical details of Imam Malik ibn Anas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key aspects of Imam Malik's life, from his noble lineage and miraculous birth to his distinguished teaching career and habits.

Core Idea
Imam Malik's noble lineage and extraordinary character foretold his role as the scholar of Medina.

This passage introduces us to Imam Malik ibn Anas, one of the four great imams of Sunni Islam. His biography is not merely a list of dates and names; it reveals the spiritual and intellectual atmosphere that produced a scholar whose fame spread across the Muslim world. The author, al-Sawi, begins by placing Imam Malik within the broader classification of scholars before detailing his remarkable ancestry and personal habits.

Guided Path
1
Situate Imam Malik
Understand how al-Sawi categorizes Malik among the scholarly groups: those who expound the beliefs of the Salaf and those who engage in spiritual works.
2
Examine his lineage
Note the meaning of his name, his tribal affiliation as al-Asbahi, and his status as a mawla 'ahd (client of covenant) indicating noble lineage.
His lineage connects to the royal houses of Himyar.
3
Reflect on his miraculous birth
His mother carried him for three years, a sign of his exceptional intellect and future greatness.
This fact is explicitly mentioned and tied to his abundant intellect.
4
Explore his family
Learn about his father Anas (a jurist), his grandfather Malik (a Follower who buried 'Uthman), and his great-grandfather Abu 'Amir (a Companion who attended all battles except Badr).
5
Learn his teaching methods
Understand his habits before teaching (ablution, prayer, perfume), his prohibition of loud voices, and his statement about not speaking to those who do not obey.
He taught for 70 years and began at age 17.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفرقة نصبت نفسها للاشتغال ببيان العقائد التي كان عليها السلف، وهم الأشعري والماتريدي ومن تبعهما. وفرقة نصبت نفسها للاشتغال بالعمل والمجاهدات على طبق ما ذهب إليه الفرقتان المتقدمتان، وهم الأئمة الأربعة. وفرقة نصبت نفسها للاشتغال بالعمل والمجاهدات على طبق ما ذهب إليه الفرقتان المتقدمتان، وهم الإمام مالك. مالك، بفتح الميم، فمثناة تحتية - الأصبحي، بفتح الباء؛ نسبة إلى ذي أصبح، بطن من حمير وهو من العرب، عهده في قريش في بني تيم الله، فهو مولى عهد لا مولى عتاقة عند الجمهور، فهو من بيوت الملوك؛ لأنَّ القاعدة عند العرب إذا جاؤوا في النَّسب بذي يكون من ذلك، حملت به أمه ثلاث سنين، وقيل: أكثر، وطول الحمل علامة على وفور عقل المولود. ولد سنة ثلاث وتسعين من الهجرة على الأشهر بذي المروءة، موضع من مساجد تبوك على ثمانية بُرد من المدينة ولا ينافيه قول عياض: إنّه الدار والمولد والمنشأ؛ لأنَّ ذا المروءة من أعمال المدينة، وقيل: ولد سنة تسعين ومات سنة تسع وسبعين ومئة، ودفن بالبقيع وقبره مدني مشهور. وكان أنس أبوه فقيهاً، وجده مالك كان من كبار التابعين، أحد الأربعة الذين حملوا عثمان إلى قبره ليلاً وغسلوه ودفنوه. وجده أبو عامر صحابي حضر مع المصطفى مغازيه كلَّها إلا بدراً. ومالك من أتباع التّابعين على الصَّحيح، وقيل: من التابعين؛ لإدراكه عائشة بنت سعد بن أبي وقاص وهي صحابية، والصحيح أنَّها تابعيَّة. وأخذ العلم عن سبع مئة شيخ؛ منهم ثلاث مئة من التّابعين، وعليه حمل قوله: «لا تنقضي السَّاعة حتّى تضرب أكباد الإبل من كل ناحية إلى عالم المدينة يطلبون علمه» وفي رواية: «يوشك أن تضرب أكباد الإبل يطلبون العلم فلا يجدون أحداً أعلم من عالم المدينة». فكانوا يزدحمون على بابه لطلب العلم وأفتى النَّاس وعلمهم نحو سبعين سنة بالمدينة، ومكث خمساً وعشرين سنة لم يشهد جماعة، فقيل له: ما يمنعك من الخروج؟ فقال: «إنَّ من الأعذار أعذاراً لا تذكر». وجلس للتدريس وهو ابن سبع عشرة سنة، وكان يقول: «لا ينبغي للعالم أن يتكلم بالعلم عند من لا يطيعه، فإنَّه ذلٌّ وإهانة للعلم». وكان إذا أراد أن يجلس للعلم؛ توضأ وصلى ركعتين، وسرَّح لحيته وتطيب، وجلس على وقار وهيبة، ومنع النَّاس من رفع أصواتهم، وبخّر.
And a group devoted itself to explaining the beliefs upon which the Salaf were, namely al-Ash'ari, al-Maturidi, and those who followed them. And a group devoted itself to works and spiritual struggles in accordance with what the two aforementioned groups held, namely the four imams. And a group devoted itself to works and spiritual struggles in accordance with what the two aforementioned groups held, namely Imam Malik. Malik, with fatha on the mim, then a dotted ya' – al-Asbahi, with fatha on the ba', in relation to Dhu Asbah, a sub-tribe of Himyar, and he is of the Arabs, his alliance in Quraysh among Banu Taym Allah, so he is a client of covenant (mawla 'ahd) not a client of emancipation (mawla 'itaqa) according to the majority, thus he is from royal houses. Because the rule among the Arabs when they use 'Dhu' in lineage is that it indicates that. His mother carried him for three years, and it is said longer, and lengthy pregnancy is a sign of the abundance of the child's intellect. He was born in the year ninety-three of the Hijra, most famously in Dhi al-Muru'a, a location of the mosques of Tabuk, eight miles (burd) from Medina. This does not contradict the statement of 'Iyad: 'It is the home, birthplace, and place of growth' because Dhi al-Muru'a is among the districts of Medina. It is said he was born in the year ninety and died in the year one hundred and seventy-nine, and was buried in al-Baqi', and his grave in Medina is well-known. His father Anas was a jurist, and his grandfather Malik was among the great Followers (tabi'un), one of the four who carried 'Uthman to his grave at night, washed him, and buried him. His great-grandfather Abu 'Amir was a Companion who attended all of the Chosen One's military campaigns except Badr. Malik was from the Followers of the Followers (atba' al-tabi'in) according to the correct view, and it is said from the Followers (tabi'un) because he met 'A'isha bint Sa'd ibn Abi Waqqas, who was a Companion, but the correct view is that she was a Follower (tabi'iyya). He acquired knowledge from seven hundred shaykhs, among them three hundred from the Followers. Upon this is based his statement: 'The Hour will not come until camels' humps are struck from every direction to the scholar of Medina seeking his knowledge.' In another narration: 'It is soon that camels' humps will be struck seeking knowledge, and they will find no one more knowledgeable than the scholar of Medina.' So they crowded at his door seeking knowledge. He issued fatwas and taught people for about seventy years in Medina. He remained for twenty-five years not attending the congregational prayer, so it was said to him: 'What prevents you from coming out?' He said: 'Among excuses are excuses that are not mentioned.' He sat for teaching while he was seventeen years old. He used to say: 'It is not fitting for a scholar to speak knowledge to one who does not obey him, for that is humiliation and disrespect for knowledge.' When he wanted to sit for knowledge, he performed ablution, prayed two rak'ats, combed his beard, applied perfume, sat with dignity and awe, prevented people from raising their voices, and fumigated.
Source pages p. 442

Commentary
This passage provides a biographical sketch of Imam Malik ibn Anas, one of the four great imams of Sunni Islam. It begins by placing him within the scholarly groupings: those who expound the beliefs of the Salaf (al-Ash'ari, al-Maturidi) and those who engage in works and spiritual struggles, namely the four imams, including Malik. The author then details Malik's lineage: his name is Malik with a fatha on the mim, al-Asbahi (in relation to Dhu Asbah, a Himyarite tribe). He is a mawla 'ahd (client of covenant) to Banu Taym Allah in Quraysh, indicating noble lineage. A peculiar fact is that his mother carried him for three years (or more), which was considered a sign of his exceptional intellect. He was born in 93 AH in Dhi al-Muru'a, near Medina. This is reconciled with 'Iyad's statement that Medina was his home. He died in 179 AH and was buried in al-Baqi', with a well-known grave. His father Anas was a jurist, his grandfather Malik was a great Follower (tabi'i) who participated in burying 'Uthman. His great-grandfather Abu 'Amir was a Companion who fought in all battles except Badr. Imam Malik himself is correctly considered from the Followers of the Followers (atba' al-tabi'in), though some say he was a Follower because he met 'A'isha bint Sa'd, who was a Companion but correctly considered a Follower. He studied under 700 shaykhs, 300 of them from the Followers. The famous hadith about a scholar of Medina is applied to him: people will travel to him seeking knowledge. He taught and issued fatwas for about 70 years. He did not attend congregational prayer for 25 years due to valid excuses he kept private. He began teaching at age 17. He emphasized that a scholar should not speak knowledge to those who do not obey him, as it degrades knowledge. Before teaching, he would perform ablution, pray, groom, perfume, and sit with dignity, forbidding loud voices and fumigating the place.

الأصبحي
ذي المروءة

أتباع التابعين
مولى عهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imam Malik was a Companion (sahabi) or that his prolonged pregnancy was purely mythical.
Correction: He was from the Followers of the Followers (atba' al-tabi'in). His grandfather was a Follower and his great-grandfather was a Companion. The prolonged pregnancy is a sign of his intellect, not a myth.

The Scholar of Medina: Malik's Teachers and the Prophetic Proclamation
Malik's teachers and the prophecy of the scholar of Medina.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the significance of Imam Malik's extensive education under seven hundred teachers and how a prophetic hadith designated him as the 'scholar of Medina.'

Core Idea
Imam Malik's knowledge was so vast that the Prophet himself foretold people would travel to seek him.

This passage opens by highlighting the breadth of Imam Malik's education—seven hundred teachers, including three hundred from the generation after the Companions. It then connects this to a famous prophetic prophecy about the 'scholar of Medina,' confirming Malik's extraordinary status. Let's explore how this establishes his authority.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the specific numbers mentioned.
Pay attention to the two versions of the hadith.
2
Identify the key figures
Observe that Imam Malik had 700 teachers, 300 of whom were from the Tabi'un. This shows his deep roots in early Islamic scholarship.
3
Connect to the prophetic hadith
Reflect on how this hadith predicts people traveling to the 'scholar of Medina' and how Malik's life fulfilled that prophecy.
This hadith is a strong endorsement of Malik's status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأخذ العلم عن سبع مئة شيخ؛ منهم ثلاث مئة من التّابعين، وعليه حمل قوله: «لا تنقضي السَّاعة حتّى تضرب أكباد الإبل من كل ناحية إلى عالم المدينة يطلبون علمه» وفي رواية: «يوشك أن تضرب أكباد الإبل يطلبون العلم فلا يجدون أحداً أعلم من عالم المدينة» فكانوا يزدحمون على بابه لطلب العلم وأفتى النَّاس وعلمهم نحو سبعين سنة بالمدينة، ومكث خمساً وعشرين سنة لم يشهد جماعة، فقيل له: ما يمنعك من الخروج؟ فقال: إنَّ من الأعذار أعذاراً لا تذكر. وجلس للتدريس وهو ابن سبع عشرة سنة، وكان يقول: لا ينبغي للعالم أن يتكلم بالعلم عند من لا يطيعه، فإنَّه ذلٌّ وإهانة للعلم. وكان إذا أراد أن يجلس للعلم؛ توضأ وصلى ركعتين، وسرَّح لحيته وتطيب، وجلس على وقار وهيبة، ومنع النَّاس من رفع أصواتهم، وبخّر المجلس بعود. وقال عبد الله بن المبارك: كنت عند الإمام مالك بن أنس وهو يحدث بحديث رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، فلدغته عقرب ستّ عشرة مرة وهو يصفر ويتلوى ولا يقطع حديث رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، فسألته عن ذلك، فقال: إنَّما صبرت إجلالاً لحديثه. وكان مهاباً جداً، إذا أجاب في مسألة لا يمكن أن يقال له: من أين؟ وكان يرى المصطفى صلى الله عليه وسلم له كل ليلة في النوم، وكان يرخي الطيلسان على رأسه، حتَّى لا يرى ولا يُرى، وكان لا يدخل الخلاء إلَّا كلَّ ثلاثة أيَّام مرة، ويقول: والله؛ لقد استحيت من الله في كثرة تردُّدي للخلاء. وقال أشهب بن عبد العزيز: رأيت أبا حنيفة بين يدي مالك، كالصَّبي بين يدي أمه. وسئل أبو حنيفة عن مالك فقال: ما رأيت أعلم بسنة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم منه.
And he acquired knowledge from seven hundred teachers; among them three hundred from the Followers (al-Tabi'un), and upon this is based his saying: 'The Hour will not pass until the camels' flanks are struck from every direction to the scholar of Medina seeking his knowledge.' In another narration: 'It is soon that the camels' flanks will be struck seeking knowledge, and they will not find anyone more knowledgeable than the scholar of Medina.' So they would crowd at his door seeking knowledge, and he gave religious verdicts to the people and taught them for about seventy years in Medina, and he stayed for twenty-five years without attending the congregation prayer, so it was said to him: What prevents you from going out? He said: Indeed among excuses are excuses that are not mentioned. And he sat for teaching while he was seventeen years old, and he used to say: It is not proper for a scholar to speak knowledge before those who do not obey him, for that is humiliation and disrespect for knowledge. And when he wanted to sit for knowledge, he would perform ablution and pray two rak'as, comb his beard and apply perfume, sit with dignity and awe, prevent people from raising their voices, and fumigate the gathering with aloeswood. And Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak said: I was with Imam Malik ibn Anas while he was narrating a hadith of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, and a scorpion stung him sixteen times while he was whistling and writhing, and he did not interrupt the hadith of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace. So I asked him about that, and he said: I only endured out of reverence for his hadith. And he was greatly feared; when he answered a question, it was not possible to say to him: From where? And he used to see the Chosen One, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, every night in his sleep, and he would let down the tailan (a hooded cloak) over his head so that he neither saw nor was seen, and he would not enter the lavatory except once every three days, saying: By Allah, I am ashamed before Allah due to my frequent going to the lavatory. And Ashhab ibn Abd al-Aziz said: I saw Abu Hanifa before Malik, like a child before his mother. And Abu Hanifa was asked about Malik and said: I have not seen anyone more knowledgeable in the Sunna of the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, than him.
Source pages p. 443

Commentary
This passage is a biographical account of Imam Malik ibn Anas, focusing on his education, teaching methods, and personal virtues. It begins by noting that he studied under seven hundred teachers, including three hundred from the generation following the Companions (Tabi'un). This vast number underscores his extensive learning and authority. The author then connects this to a hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ) about people traveling to the 'scholar of Medina'—interpreted as Malik—to seek knowledge, confirming his reputation. Next, it describes the crowds that gathered at his door for knowledge and his long career of teaching and issuing fatwas for about seventy years. It mentions that for twenty-five years he did not attend the congregational prayer, and when asked why, he replied that some excuses are better left unspoken, implying valid personal reasons. The passage then highlights his early start in teaching at age seventeen and his principle that a scholar should not present knowledge to those who do not respect it, as this demeans knowledge. His preparation for teaching sessions included ritual purification, prayer, grooming, and perfuming, and he maintained an atmosphere of dignity, preventing loud voices and fumigating the room. A famous story with Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak illustrates his profound reverence for the Prophet's hadith: while narrating, a scorpion stung him sixteen times, yet he did not stop out of respect. His fear-inspiring dignity meant that when he gave an answer, no one dared ask for evidence. He also saw the Prophet (ﷺ) in his dreams every night—a sign of his spiritual rank. He covered his head so as not to be seen, and he limited his visits to the lavatory to once every three days, feeling ashamed before Allah for frequenting it. Finally, the passage records the high praise of Abu Hanifa, who is described as standing before Malik like a child before his mother, and who declared Malik the most knowledgeable in the Sunna. This account cements Malik's stature as a paragon of knowledge, piety, and reverence for the prophetic tradition.

Key Terms
عالم المدينة
طلب العلم
التابعين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Imam Malik had only a few teachers or was self-taught.
Correction: The passage states he had seven hundred teachers, indicating a vast and rigorous education.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many of Imam Malik's teachers were from the Tabi'un?
A) 300
B) 700
C) 500
D) 200

Key Takeaway
Imam Malik's seven hundred teachers and the Prophet's prophecy affirm his unparalleled knowledge and his role as the scholar of Medina.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Imam Malik studied under 700 teachers. How many teachers have you personally sat with to learn your religion? Are you truly seeking knowledge, or are you just acquiring information passively? Commit today to find one teacher to learn from regularly.
?
The Prophet foretold that people would travel far to learn from Malik. How far are you willing to go—physically or digitally—to find authentic knowledge? What excuses have you been making? Write down one concrete step you will take this week.
?
Malik taught and gave fatwas for seventy years. Imagine your life at that age—what legacy of knowledge will you leave behind? If you were to die tomorrow, how many people would have learned from you? Start today, even if it is teaching one person one verse.

Life of Imam al-Sawi
beginner

Malik's Teaching Conduct: Sacrifice, Preparation, and Reverence
Malik's teaching habits, conditions, and the scorpion incident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Malik's approach to teaching, including his conditions for students, his personal preparation, and his profound reverence for the Prophet's hadith as shown by the scorpion incident.

Core Idea
For Imam Malik, the dignity of knowledge was paramount, even to the point of enduring physical pain.

This passage delves into Imam Malik's teaching principles and practices. It recounts his early start, his insistence on respectful students, his meticulous preparation, and the famous story of his endurance during a scorpion sting while narrating hadith. Let's see how these details reveal his character.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the sequence: his absence from congregation, early teaching, conditions, preparation, and the scorpion story.
The scorpion story is a dramatic highlight.
2
Analyze his teaching criteria
He did not teach those who would not obey him, and he prepared ritually and physically before sessions. This shows his high regard for knowledge.
3
Reflect on the scorpion incident
Why did he not stop? His answer reveals his awe for the Prophet's words over his own pain.
This demonstrates his epitome of adab (etiquette) towards hadith.

Why did Imam Malik not stop narrating hadith even after being stung by a scorpion sixteen times?
A) He did not feel the pain.
B) He was too focused on the hadith.

D) The scorpion was not venomous.

Imam Malik's teaching conduct reflects his deep reverence for knowledge and the Prophet's hadith, even beyond personal comfort.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you prepare to learn or share Islamic knowledge, do you take even one deliberate step to show reverence—like wudu or a short prayer—or do you treat it like ordinary information? What would change if you started each study session with a moment of sacred intention?
?
Have you ever held back from speaking about Islam because you sensed the listener was not in a state of respect? Why did you choose that, and how can you balance protecting the dignity of knowledge with compassionate invitation?
?
Imagine you were stung multiple times while trying to convey a hadith. Would your first thought be to stop or to endure for the sake of the Prophet's words? What does your answer reveal about the depth of your love for the sunnah? What step can you take this week to increase that love?

Malik's Status: Awe, Dreams, and Peer Recognition
Malik's awe, dreams, modesty, and recognition by Abu Hanifa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the signs of Imam Malik's elevated spiritual status, including his awe-inspiring presence, his dreams of the Prophet, and the high praise of Imam Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
Imam Malik was so revered that even Abu Hanifa stood before him like a child.

This final part of the biography describes Malik's majestic demeanor, his regular dreams of the Prophet, his extreme modesty even in bodily functions, and the extraordinary tribute from Abu Hanifa. These details cement his stature as a towering figure in Islamic scholarship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Note the four features: his authority, dreams, modesty, and peer praise.
The dream of the Prophet is a high spiritual blessing.
2
Consider his awe and dreams
No one could challenge his answers, and he saw the Prophet every night. This indicates his spiritual rank.
3
Reflect on Abu Hanifa's statement
Abu Hanifa, a great scholar himself, acknowledging Malik's superiority is a powerful testimony.
This shows the unity and mutual respect among early imams.

What did Abu Hanifa say about Imam Malik's knowledge?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you feel that same awe for Allah that Malik felt, to the point that you are ashamed of even your natural needs? What does your attitude towards your own bodily functions say about your consciousness of Allah's presence?

Malik saw the Prophet every night. Do you ever long for a vision of the Prophet? What is preventing that from being a reality in your life, and what steps can you take to increase your love and closeness to him?
?
Abu Hanifa, a giant of knowledge, humbled himself before Malik like a child. Who do you look up to in humility? Are you willing to sit before someone greater with the humility of a child, acknowledging their superiority? If not, what pride is holding you back?

Imam Malik's Piety and Reverence for Hadith
Anecdotes demonstrating Imam Malik's reverence for hadith and personal piety.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Malik's profound reverence for the Prophet's hadith and his personal piety manifested in extraordinary patience and modest behavior.

Core Idea
Malik's reverence for hadith made him endure scorpion stings without stopping.

Imam Malik is renowned not only for his legal scholarship but also for his deep spiritual devotion. The first anecdote from Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak illustrates how Malik prioritized the sanctity of prophetic hadith over his own physical suffering. This lesson explores that story and other examples of Malik's piety, including his nightly visions of the Prophet and extreme modesty.

Guided Path
1
Read the scorpion story
Read the passage where Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak describes Malik being stung sixteen times while teaching. Notice his reason for enduring.
The number sixteen emphasizes the severity of the test.
2
Reflect on Malik's answer
Malik said he endured out of reverence for the Prophet's hadith. Consider what this says about his view of prophetic traditions.
3
Study his additional virtues
Read about his awe-inspiring presence, dreams of the Prophet, covering his head, and infrequent visits to the toilet. Each shows a different aspect of his piety.
The dreams indicate his spiritual station.
4
Connect to overall character
Ask yourself how these anecdotes together paint a picture of Imam Malik's personality and why they are included in a theological text.
This reinforces the importance of adab in Islamic scholarship.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأئمة الأربعة
المجلس بعود
وقال عبد الله بن المبارك : كنت عند الإمام مالك بن أنس وهو يحدث بحديث رسول الله ، فلدغته عقرب ستّ عشرة مرة وهو يصفر ويتلوى ولا يقطع حديث رسول الله ، فسألته عن ذلك، فقال: (إنَّما صبرت صلى الله عليه وسلم، إجلالاً لحديثه).
وكان مهاباً جداً، إذا أجاب في مسألة لا يمكن أن يقال له: من أين؟
وكان يرى المصطفى له كل ليلة في النوم، وكان يرخي الطيلسان على رأسه، حتَّى لا يرى ولا يُرى، وكان لا يدخل الخلاء إلَّا كلَّ ثلاثة أيَّام مرة، ويقول: (والله؛ لقد استحيت من الله في كثرة تردُّدي للخلاء). وقال أشهب بن عبد العزيز: (رأيت أبا حنيفة بين يدي مالك، كالصَّبي بين يدي أمه).
وسئل أبو حنيفة عن مالك فقال: (ما رأيت أعلم بسنة رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم منه).
وقال الليث بن سعد: لقيت مالكاً بالمدينة، فقلت له: ما لك تمسح العرق عن جبينك؟ فقال: (عرقت مع أبي حنيفة، إنَّه لفقيه يا مصري). ثمَّ لقيت أبا حنيفة فقلت له: ما أحسن قول مالك فيك؟ فقال له: (والله؛ ما رأيت أسرع بجواب صادق وزهد تام من مالك بن أنس).
The Four Imams
Session of return
And Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak said: I was with Imam Malik ibn Anas while he was narrating a hadith of the Messenger of God, and a scorpion stung him sixteen times while he was turning pale and writhing, and he did not interrupt the hadith of the Messenger of God. So I asked him about that, and he said: (I only endured, may God bless him and grant him peace, out of reverence for his hadith).
And he was greatly awe-inspiring; when he answered a question, it was not possible to say to him: From where?
And he used to see the Chosen One (the Prophet) every night in his sleep, and he used to let his tailasan hang down over his head so that he could not see and could not be seen, and he would not enter the toilet except once every three days, and he would say: (By God; I have become shy of God due to my frequent entering of the toilet). And Ashhab ibn Abd al-Aziz said: (I saw Abu Hanifa before Malik, like a child before his mother).
And Abu Hanifa was asked about Malik, and he said: (I have not seen anyone more knowledgeable of the Sunnah of the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, than him).
And al-Layth ibn Sa'd said: I met Malik in Medina and said to him: Why are you wiping the sweat from your forehead? He said: (I sweated with Abu Hanifa; indeed, he is a jurist, O Egyptian). Then I met Abu Hanifa and said to him: How good is Malik's statement about you? He replied: (By God; I have not seen anyone quicker with a truthful answer and more perfect in asceticism than Malik ibn Anas).
Source pages p. 444

Commentary
This passage is a collection of anecdotes highlighting the virtues of Imam Malik, his deep reverence for the Prophet's hadith, his piety, and the mutual respect between him and Abu Hanifa. The passage begins with a striking story: Abdullah ibn al-Mubarak observed Malik being stung sixteen times by a scorpion while teaching hadith, yet he did not stop. When asked, Malik explained that he endured out of reverence for the Prophet's hadith. This shows his profound respect for the prophetic tradition. Next, we learn about Malik's awe-inspiring presence: once he answered a question, no one could ask him 'from where?' meaning his answers were so authoritative that no challenge was possible. The passage also mentions that Malik saw the Prophet in his dreams every night, indicating his high spiritual rank. He would cover his head with his tailasan to avoid being seen and would only use the toilet once every three days out of modesty before God. The narrative then shifts to the relationship between Malik and Abu Hanifa. Ashhab saw Abu Hanifa before Malik like a child before his mother, implying humility. When asked about Malik, Abu Hanifa called him the most knowledgeable of the Sunnah. Finally, al-Layth ibn Sa'd recounts meeting both men: Malik praised Abu Hanifa's jurisprudence, and Abu Hanifa praised Malik's quick answers and asceticism. This mutual admiration underscores the unity and respect among the early imams.

عبد الله بن المبارك

الإجلال

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Imam Malik felt shame before God for even entering the toilet too often. How often do you consider the presence of God in your most private moments? What would change in your daily habits if you truly felt that shame?
?
Malik saw the Prophet every night in his dreams. What would it take for you to be in a state such that the Prophet's company becomes a nightly reality? Are you living a life that invites that blessing, and if not, what is the first step you will take tonight?

Mutual Respect Between Imam Malik and Abu Hanifa
Anecdotes showing mutual respect between Imam Malik and Abu Hanifa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will appreciate the mutual respect and admiration between Imam Malik and Abu Hanifa, dispelling the misconception of rivalry.

Core Idea
Malik and Abu Hanifa held deep respect for each other, praising one another's knowledge and piety.

Although often presented as having methodological differences, Imam Malik and Abu Hanifa shared a profound mutual respect. This lesson examines three accounts that illustrate their admiration: Ashhab's observation of Abu Hanifa's humility before Malik, Abu Hanifa's praise of Malik's knowledge, and the exchange through al-Layth ibn Sa'd where both imams compliment each other.

Guided Path
1
Read the first account by Ashhab
Read Ashhab's statement that Abu Hanifa sat before Malik like a child before his mother. Notice the humility implied.
This highlights the respect Abu Hanifa showed.
2
Read Abu Hanifa's praise
When asked about Malik, Abu Hanifa called him the most knowledgeable of the Sunnah. Consider the weight of this compliment from a leading scholar.
3
Read the al-Layth ibn Sa'd account
Al-Layth relates how Malik praised Abu Hanifa's jurisprudence and Abu Hanifa praised Malik's quick answers and asceticism. Note the symmetry.
Both imams acknowledged each other's strengths.
4
Reflect on the lesson
Think about why these anecdotes are preserved. They teach us that differences in methodology do not prevent mutual respect among sincere scholars.
This is a model for respectful disagreement.
