Imam al-Shafi'i: Lineage, Early Life, and Prophetic Promise
Biography of al-Shafi'i detailing his lineage, early life, and scholarly achievements.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the noble lineage of Imam al-Shafi'i, his remarkable early education, and why he is considered the fulfillment of the hadith about a Qurayshi scholar.

Core Idea
Imam al-Shafi'i's lineage, early mastery, and fulfillment of prophecy established him as a leading scholar.

This lesson presents the biography of Imam al-Shafi'i, highlighting his Qurayshi lineage, his extraordinary early education, and the prophetic hadith that identifies him as the scholar who would fill the earth with knowledge. You will see how his life story connects to his later influence and why he holds a unique place among the imams.

Guided Path
1
Read the biography and trace the lineage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay special attention to the lineage from al-Shafi'i back to Abd Manaf, the grandfather of the Prophet. Note how the author emphasizes his noble descent and the connection to the Companion Shafi'.
The lineage establishes his Qurayshi credentials, which is central to the later hadith.
2
Observe his early intellectual achievements
Notice the specific ages at which al-Shafi'i accomplished key milestones: memorizing the Quran at 7, the Muwatta' at 10, and receiving permission to give fatwas at 15. Reflect on the extraordinary nature of these achievements and the role of his teacher Muslim ibn Khalid.
His rapid mastery of sacred texts and early authorization to teach show his exceptional intellect and dedication.
3
Consider the hadith and its application
Read the statement that the hadith 'A scholar of Quraysh will fill the earth with knowledge' applies specifically to al-Shafi'i, as affirmed by Imam Ahmad and others. Ask yourself: Why is this identification significant? How does al-Shafi'i's later influence match the prophecy?
This connection elevates al-Shafi'i's status and shows his role in disseminating knowledge across the Muslim world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الشَّافعي : فهو أبو عبد الله محمد بن إدريس بن عباس بن عثمان بن شافع بن السائب بن عبد الله بن عبد يزيد بن هاشم بن عبد المطلب بن عبد مناف جدُّ النَّبيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم، وهو ابن عمّ المصطفى ؛ نسبة لشافع؛ لأنَّه أكرم أجداده ، ولأنَّه صحابي ابن صحابي.
ولد الشافعي بغزّة يوم وفاة أبي حنيفة، ونشأ يتيماً في حجر أمه مع قلة عيش وضيق، ثمَّ حمل إلى مكة وهو ابن سنتين ونشأ بها، وحفظ القرآن وهو ابن سبع سنين، و«الموطَّأ» وهو ابن عشر، وأذن له شيخه وهو مسلم بن خالد بالإفتاء، وهو ابن خمس عشرة سنة، وعليه حمل حديث: «عالم قريش يملأ طباق الأرض علماً» ؛ لأنَّ الكثرة والانتشار في جميع الأقطار لم يحصلا في عالم قرشيّ مثله ، قال الأئمة منهم أحمد هذا العالم هو الشَّافعي .
As for al-Shafi'i: he is Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Idris ibn Abbas ibn Uthman ibn Shafi' ibn al-Sa'ib ibn Abdullah ibn Abd Yazid ibn Hashim ibn Abd al-Muttalib ibn Abd Manaf, the grandfather of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and he is the cousin of the Chosen One; his lineage is to Shafi' because he is the most noble of his ancestors, and because he is a Companion son of a Companion.
Al-Shafi'i was born in Ghazza on the day of the death of Abu Hanifa, and he grew up as an orphan in the care of his mother with a meager and difficult life. Then he was taken to Mecca when he was two years old and grew up there. He memorized the Quran when he was seven years old, and the Muwatta' when he was ten. His teacher Muslim ibn Khalid gave him permission to issue fatwas when he was fifteen years old. And upon him is based the hadith: 'A scholar of Quraysh will fill the layers of the earth with knowledge' because the abundance and spread in all regions was not attained by any Qurayshi scholar like him. The imams, including Ahmad, said: 'This scholar is al-Shafi'i.'
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed biography of Imam al-Shafi'i. It begins with his full lineage tracing back to Abd Manaf, the grandfather of the Prophet, establishing his noble Qurayshi descent. He is called al-Shafi'i relative to his ancestor Shafi', who was a Companion and son of a Companion. The passage notes that al-Shafi'i was born in Ghazza on the same day Abu Hanifa died, which symbolically connects the two imams. He was raised an orphan in poverty, then moved to Mecca at age two. His early intellectual achievements are highlighted: he memorized the Quran by seven and Imam Malik's Muwatta by ten. At fifteen, his teacher Muslim ibn Khalid authorized him to give fatwas, indicating his early mastery. The passage then cites the prophetic hadith about a scholar from Quraysh filling the earth with knowledge, and states that this applies to al-Shafi'i because no other Qurayshi scholar achieved such widespread influence. The imams, including Ahmad ibn Hanbal, identified this scholar as al-Shafi'i.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may doubt that al-Shafi'i is the scholar referred to in the hadith, thinking it might refer to another Qurayshi scholar.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that the imams, including Ahmad ibn Hanbal, identified this scholar as al-Shafi'i. No other Qurayshi scholar achieved such widespread influence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Shafi'i memorized the Quran at seven and the Muwatta' at ten, despite being an orphan in poverty. What have you memorized or mastered recently? If nothing, what cost are you paying for comfort that he never had?
?
He was given permission to teach at fifteen because his teacher saw his readiness. Is there someone in your life whom you are dismissing despite clear signs of their capability? Or are you waiting for a permission that your own negligence has already delayed?
?
The hadith promised a Qurayshi scholar, and the imams agreed it was al-Shafi'i. Do you believe that God has a specific purpose for your lineage and circumstances, or do you treat your life as a random accident? What would change if you truly saw yourself as a fulfillment of a divine plan?

The Lineage and Kunya of Imam Abu Hanifa
Opening of Abu Hanifa's biography with genealogy and nickname origin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the full lineage of Imam Abu Hanifa, the two opinions on his ethnic origin, and the meaning behind his nickname (kunya).

Core Idea
Imam Abu Hanifa's lineage connects him to the Arabs, and his kunya honors his daughter.

Every great scholar has a story that begins with their name. Imam Abu Hanifa is no exception. In this brief passage, the author presents his full genealogy, a key debate about his ethnic background, and the origin of the nickname by which he is universally known. Let's uncover these details step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic text
Read the Arabic passage once straight through. Notice the chain of names and the quick shifts in reported opinions.
The text is only one sentence but packed with information.
2
Identify the full name
Find the four‑part genealogy: al‑Nu‘man ibn Thabit ibn Tawus ibn Hurmuz. Note that the text adds 'king of Banu Shayban' for Hurmuz.
3
Understand the ethnic debate
The text first asserts he is from the Arabs (Banu Shayban), then mentions a minority view that he is from the Persians. Notice the contrast.
This shows the author is reporting scholarly differences.
4
Examine the kunya
The kunya 'Abu Hanifa' is explained: either after his daughter Hanifa or after his inkwell (dawat). Wonder why an inkwell would be a source of a nickname.
The inkwell points to his scholarly dedication.
5
Connect to the commentary
Read the provided commentary to solidify the points. Then answer the checkpoint question to test your understanding.
The commentary repeats and clarifies the same information.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا أبو حنيفة : فهو النعمان بن ثابت بن طاووس بن هرمز ملك بنى شیبان فهو من العرب، وقيل: ومن الفرس كني ببنته، وقيل: بدواته،
As for Abu Hanifa: he is al-Nu'man ibn Thabit ibn Tawus ibn Hurmuz, king of Banu Shayban, so he is from the Arabs. And it is said: from the Persians. He was nicknamed after his daughter, and it is said: after his inkwell.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage begins the biography of Imam Abu Hanifa by giving his full name: al-Nu'man ibn Thabit ibn Tawus ibn Hurmuz. It mentions that he was from the tribe of Banu Shayban, so he was of Arab descent, though some say he was Persian. The passage also notes that his kunya (nickname) came from his daughter (Abu Hanifa means 'father of Hanifa') and some say from his inkwell (indicating his scholarly activity). The biography is cut off here in the source.

Key Terms
أبو حنيفة
النعمان بن ثابت
بنو شيبان
الفرس
الكُنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think 'Abu Hanifa' means he had a son named Hanifa.
Correction: The text explicitly says 'كني ببنته' meaning 'he was nicknamed after his daughter' (Hanifa), not a son.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Imam Abu Hanifa's full name is al-Nu‘man ibn Thabit ibn Tawus ibn Hurmuz; he was of Arab lineage from Banu Shayban, though some say Persian, and his kunya 'Abu Hanifa' comes either from his daughter or his inkwell.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage mentions conflicting reports about his lineage. In your own life, how much do you define yourself by your background or family name versus your actions and character? Does this lesson challenge you to shift your focus?

Anecdote about Malik and Abu Hanifa
Narrates a story illustrating mutual respect between Malik and Abu Hanifa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the early imams Malik and Abu Hanifa held mutual respect despite different approaches, as shown in a brief anecdote.

Core Idea
Great scholars respect each other's strengths.

This lesson presents a short anecdote that reveals the character of two of Islam's greatest jurists. It reminds us that scholarly disagreement does not preclude genuine admiration. Read the story and reflect on the qualities highlighted.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the chain of narration and the direct speech of each imam.
The narrator is al-Layth ibn Sa'd, a contemporary of both.
2
Identify the two encounters
First, al-Layth meets Malik and asks why he is sweating. Then he meets Abu Hanifa and relays Malik's praise. Notice the response of each imam.
3
Reflect on the mutual praise
Malik calls Abu Hanifa a 'faqih' (jurist). Abu Hanifa praises Malik's quick truthful answers and complete asceticism. What does this teach about their characters?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الليث بن سعد : لقيت مالكاً بالمدينة، فقلت له : ما لك تمسح العرق عن جبينك ؟ فقال : عرقت مع أبي حنيفة، إنَّه لفقيه يا مصري، ثمَّ لقيت أبا حنيفة فقلت :له ما أحسن قول مالك فيك؟ فقال له : والله ؛ ما رأيت أسرع بجواب صادق وزهد تام من مالك بن أنس) (۱) .
And al-Layth ibn Sa'd said: I met Malik in Medina, so I said to him: Why are you wiping sweat from your forehead? He said: I sweated with Abu Hanifa; indeed he is a faqih (jurist), O Egyptian. Then I met Abu Hanifa and said to him: How good was Malik's statement about you? So he said to him: By Allah, I have not seen anyone quicker with a truthful answer and more complete in zuhd (asceticism) than Malik ibn Anas.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is a brief anecdote that illustrates the mutual respect between the two great imams, Malik and Abu Hanifa. It is narrated by al-Layth ibn Sa'd. First, al-Layth meets Malik in Medina and notices him wiping sweat from his forehead. Malik explains that he was in discussion with Abu Hanifa, calling him a faqih. Then al-Layth meets Abu Hanifa and tells him about Malik's praise. Abu Hanifa responds by praising Malik's swift truthful answers and complete asceticism. The story highlights the admirable qualities of both imams, reinforcing the unity and mutual recognition among the early scholars.

Key Terms
مالك
أبو حنيفة
فقيه
زهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Malik and Abu Hanifa were rivals or had enmity.
Correction: This anecdote shows Malik praised Abu Hanifa's jurisprudence and Abu Hanifa praised Malik's quick answers and asceticism.

Checkpoint
What reason does Malik give for sweating profusely?
A) He was ill
B) He was engaged in discussion with Abu Hanifa
C) He had been traveling
D) He was performing physical labor

Key Takeaway
This anecdote shows that even great imams respected each other's expertise and character.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you honestly praised someone you disagree with for a quality you admire? What stops you from doing it more often, and what one concrete step will you take this week to overcome that barrier?
?
Malik said he 'sweated' with Abu Hanifa — a sign of intense engagement. Do you avoid difficult discussions with those who differ from you? What might your avoidance reveal about your attachment to comfort rather than truth?
?
If the greatest imams could speak so highly of each other despite their differences, what does your own reluctance to acknowledge others' virtues say about your heart? Write down one truthful compliment for someone you have previously criticized, and commit to delivering it before the end of the week.

The Noble Lineage and Early Life of Imam al-Shafi'i
Details the lineage, birth, upbringing, and scholarly status of Imam al-Shafi'i.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the noble lineage, early upbringing, and exceptional scholarly achievements of Imam al-Shafi'i as a model of early Islamic learning.

Core Idea
Imam al-Shafi'i's noble descent and early mastery of knowledge prefigured his role as a leading scholar.

This lesson introduces the life of Imam al-Shafi'i, whose lineage traces back to the Prophet's own family. His early memorization, authorization to teach, and recognition by later scholars mark him as a unique figure in Islamic history.

Guided Path
1
Read the biographical passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of Imam al-Shafi'i's life from lineage to scholarly recognition.
Notice how the passage begins with his full lineage, establishing his noble Qurayshi descent.
2
Identify key life milestones
List the major events: birth, move to Mecca, memorization of Quran and Muwatta, authorization for fatwa at fifteen.
These milestones show his exceptional intellectual development from a young age.
3
Understand the hadith about a Qurayshi scholar
Reflect on the hadith that a scholar of Quraysh will fill the earth with knowledge, and how the imams identified this scholar as al-Shafi'i.
This connects his personal achievements to a prophetic prophecy.
4
Consider the significance of his early achievements
Think about why memorizing the Quran at seven and Muwatta at ten, and giving fatwas at fifteen, were remarkable for his time.
This sets the stage for his later founding of a major school of law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا الشَّافعي : فهو أبو عبد الله محمد بن إدريس بن عباس بن عثمان بن شافع بن السائب بن عبد الله بن عبد يزيد بن هاشم بن عبد المطلب بن عبد مناف جدُّ النَّبيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم، وهو ابن عمّ المصطفى ؛ نسبة لشافع؛ لأنَّه أكرم أجداده ، ولأنَّه صحابي ابن صحابي. ولد الشافعي بغزّة يوم وفاة أبي حنيفة، ونشأ يتيماً في حجر أمه مع قلة عيش وضيق، ثمَّ حمل إلى مكة وهو ابن سنتين ونشأ بها، وحفظ القرآن وهو ابن سبع سنين، و«الموطَّأ وهو ابن عشر، وأذن له شيخه وهو مسلم بن خالد بالإفتاء، وهو ابن خمس عشرة سنة، وعليه حمل حديث: عالم قريش يملأ طباق الأرض علماً » (۲) ؛ لأنَّ الكثرة والانتشار في جميع الأقطار لم يحصلا في عالم قرشيّ مثله ، قال الأئمة منهم أحمد هذا العالم هو الشَّافعي .
As for al-Shafi'i: he is Abu Abdullah Muhammad ibn Idris ibn Abbas ibn Uthman ibn Shafi' ibn al-Sa'ib ibn Abdullah ibn Abd Yazid ibn Hashim ibn Abd al-Muttalib ibn Abd Manaf, the grandfather of the Prophet (peace be upon him), and he is the cousin of the Chosen One (al-Mustafa); his lineage is attributed to Shafi' because he is the most noble of his ancestors, and because he is a Companion, son of a Companion. Al-Shafi'i was born in Ghazza on the day of Abu Hanifa's death, and grew up an orphan under the care of his mother in straitened circumstances. Then he was taken to Mecca at the age of two and grew up there. He memorized the Quran at seven, and al-Muwatta' at ten. His teacher, Muslim ibn Khalid, authorized him to give fatwas at fifteen. And upon him is applied the hadith: 'A scholar of Quraysh will fill the earth with knowledge,' because the abundance and spread in all regions has not occurred in a Qurayshi scholar like him. The imams, among them Ahmad, said: 'This scholar is al-Shafi'i.'
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage is a biographical sketch of Imam al-Shafi'i. It begins with his full lineage, tracing back to Abd Manaf, the grandfather of the Prophet, establishing his noble Qurayshi descent. It notes that he is a cousin of the Prophet and that his ancestor Shafi' was a Companion. The passage then describes his birth in Ghazza on the day of Abu Hanifa's death, his orphaned childhood, and early hardship. He was taken to Mecca at age two and grew up there. His exceptional intellectual abilities are highlighted: he memorized the Quran at seven and al-Muwatta' (Malik's hadith collection) at ten. At fifteen, his teacher Muslim ibn Khalid authorized him to issue fatwas. Finally, the passage connects him to the prophetic hadith praising a Qurayshi scholar who would spread knowledge, and reports that the scholars, including Imam Ahmad, identified that scholar as al-Shafi'i.

Key Terms
الشافعي
قرشي
الموطأ
الإفتاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Ten
C) Fifteen
D) Twenty

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Shafi'i's noble lineage and exceptional early mastery of Quran and hadith prepared him to become one of the greatest scholars, fulfilling the prophecy of a Qurayshi scholar spreading knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Shafi'i memorized the Quran at seven while an orphan in poverty. You have likely had more resources and comfort. What specific step will you take this week to prioritize Quran memorization or study, knowing that your excuses are weaker than his hardships?
?
He was authorized to give fatwa at fifteen through extraordinary dedication. At your current stage in life, what scholarly or spiritual goal have you set that matches his level of commitment? If none, why not?
?
His noble lineage did not exempt him from hard work; it only added responsibility. Are you relying on your family's religious reputation or your own efforts? What will change today?

Biography of Imam Abu Hanifa: Lineage and Companionship
Biographical passage about Abu Hanifa's lineage and Companions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Imam Abu Hanifa's lineage, ethnic origin, and the specific Companions from whom he heard hadith.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa's direct connection to the Companions strengthens his scholarly authority.

This passage begins the biography of Imam Abu Hanifa, detailing his lineage and his connection to the Companions. Knowing that he met about twenty Companions and heard hadith from nine of them provides a crucial link to the prophetic era. This lesson presents that foundational information.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Let the text's rhythm come through before interpreting.
Notice the list of names at the end.
2
Identify the biographical details
Note the full name, lineage, and the discussion about his Arab or Persian origin. Also note the explanation of his kunya (Abu Hanifa).
The lineage traces back to the Banu Shayban tribe.
3
Focus on the Companions
The passage states he met about twenty Companions and lists nine from whom he heard hadith. Identify each name and its significance.
Some names may be unfamiliar; research them if needed.
4
Reflect on the importance
Ask yourself why this connection matters for Abu Hanifa's authority and for the transmission of hadith and fiqh.
This connects to the overall goal of understanding early Islamic scholarship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا أبو حنيفة : فهو النعمان بن ثابت بن طاووس بن هرمز ملك بنى شیبان فهو من العرب، وقيل: ومن الفرس كني ببنته، وقيل: بدواته، ذكر جماعة أنه أدرك نحو عشرين صحابياً، وسمع الحديث من تسعة منهم، وهم أنس بن مالك وعمرو بن حريث، وعبد الله بن أنس، وعبد الله بن الحارث، وجابر بن عبد الله بن أبي أوفى ووائلة بن الأسقع، ومعقل بن يسار، وأبو الطفيل عامر، وعائشة بنت عجرة. هلال بن
As for Abu Hanifa: he is al-Nu'man ibn Thabit ibn Tawus ibn Hurmuz, king of the Banu Shayban; he is from the Arabs, and it is said also from the Persians. He was called by his daughter's name (Abu Hanifa), and it is said by his inkwell. A group mentioned that he met about twenty Companions, and heard hadith from nine of them: Anas ibn Malik, Amr ibn Hurayth, Abdullah ibn Anas, Abdullah ibn al-Harith, Jabir ibn Abdullah, ibn Abi Awfa, Wa'ila ibn al-Asqa', Ma'qil ibn Yasar, Abu al-Tufayl 'Amir, and 'A'isha bint 'Ajra. Hilal ibn.
Source pages p. 445

Commentary
This passage provides the biography of Imam Abu Hanifa. It begins with his full name and lineage, tracing to the Banu Shayban tribe. There is a note that he was of Arab origin, with some saying also Persian. His kunya (Abu Hanifa) is explained either from his daughter or from his inkwell. The passage highlights his connection to the Companions: he met about twenty Companions and specifically heard hadith from nine of them, who are listed. This list underscores his direct link to the prophetic generation, lending authority to his scholarship. The final name 'Hilal ibn' is incomplete in the text.

النعمان
صحابي
حديث

Misconception: Imam Abu Hanifa never met any Companions directly.
Correction: The text explicitly states he met about twenty Companions and heard hadith from nine, including major figures like Anas ibn Malik.

Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal: Lineage, Piety, and Poetic Mnemonic
Biography of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal with poetic lifespan mnemonic for the four imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the biography of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, his relationship with al-Shafi'i, his devotional practice, and the poetic mnemonic used to remember the lifespans of the four famous imams.

Core Idea
A student of al-Shafi'i known for unmatched piety and a poetic code for the imams' dates.

This lesson introduces Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, one of the four great Sunni imams. After recounting his lineage and the high praise of his teacher al-Shafi'i, the author includes a unique poetic mnemonic that encodes the birth and death years of the four imams. As you study the passage, note how the poem uses abjad numerals to preserve historical data in a memorable form.

Guided Path
1
Read Ahmad's biography
Read the Arabic passage once to get an overview of Ahmad's lineage, birth, and relationship with al-Shafi'i.
Notice how his lineage traces back to Nizar, connecting him to the Prophet.
2
Understand al-Shafi'i's testimonial
Focus on the quotation from al-Shafi'i, which praises Ahmad's knowledge, piety, and asceticism.
This shows the high regard even his teacher had for him.
3
Appreciate Ahmad's devotion
Note that Ahmad would stay awake at night and complete a full recitation of the Quran daily—a sign of extreme piety.
4
Decode the poetic mnemonic
Read the rajaz poem and the explanation that follows. Understand how words encode years using abjad numerals, using Abu Hanifa's data as an example.
The poem provides a memorable way to recall historical dates; the exact abjad values may vary but the concept is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا أحمد بن حنبل فهو أبو عبد الله أحمد بن محمد بن حنبل : أسد المروزيُّ الشَّيبانيُّ، يجتمع مع النَّبيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم في نزار بن معد بن عدنان البغدادي، قدمت به أمه من مروز وهي حاملة به فولدته ببغداد، وهو تلميذ الشافعي، قال الشافعي : خرجت من بغداد وما خلفت فيها أفقه ولا أورع ولا أزهد ولا أعلم من الإمام أحمد بن حنبل))، وكان يحيي اللَّيل لله من وقت كونه غلاماً، وله في كل يوم وليلة ختم. كلَّه وفضل هؤلاء الأئمة أشهر من الشَّمس في رابعة النَّهار، ونظم بعضهم تاريخ ولادة الأربعة ووفاتهم ومدة عمرهم بقوله (۲) : [من الرجز] تاریخ نعمان یکن سیف سطا ومالك في قطع جوف ضبطا والشَّافعي صــيــن بــبــرنــد وأحـمـد بـسـبـق أمر جعد فاحسب على ترتيب نظم الشعر میلادهم فموتهم كالعمر فولادة أبي حنيفة سنة ثمانين وجمله يكن)، ووفاته سنة مئة وخمسين وجمله (سيف)، وعمره سبعون وجمله (سطا).
As for Ahmad ibn Hanbal: he is Abu Abdullah Ahmad ibn Muhammad ibn Hanbal, the lion of Marw, al-Shaybani. He meets with the Prophet (peace be upon him) in Nizar ibn Ma'd ibn Adnan, the Baghdadi. His mother brought him from Marw while pregnant, and she gave birth to him in Baghdad. He is a student of al-Shafi'i. Al-Shafi'i said: 'I left Baghdad and I did not leave behind in it anyone more knowledgeable, more pious, more ascetic, or more learned than Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal.' He used to stay awake at night for Allah from the time he was a youth, and he would complete a recitation of the Quran every day and night. All of this. And the virtue of these imams is more famous than the sun in the midday sky. One of them composed the dates of the birth and death of the four and their lifespans in his verse: [from rajaz] The date of Nu'man is 'yakun' and 'sayf' struck, and Malik in 'qati' jauf' was accurate, and al-Shafi'i is 'sayn' by 'burund', and Ahmad by 'sabiqa' amr ja'dun'. So calculate according to the order of the poetry their births, then their deaths as the lifespan. So the birth of Abu Hanifa is the year eighty and its phrase is 'yakun', and his death is the year one hundred and fifty and its phrase is 'sayf', and his lifespan is seventy and its phrase is 'sata'.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage presents the biography of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal. It gives his name, lineage tracing to Nizar, and notes he was born in Baghdad after his mother traveled from Marw while pregnant. He is identified as a student of al-Shafi'i, and a quotation from al-Shafi'i praises his knowledge, piety, and asceticism. The passage highlights Ahmad's devotion: he would stay awake at night in worship and complete a full recitation of the Quran daily. Then it transitions to a general statement about the fame of the four imams. Finally, it includes a poetic mnemonic (rajaz) that encodes the birth and death years of each imam using abjad numerals. The poem is explained: for Abu Hanifa, the word 'yakun' gives the birth year 80 AH (since 'ya'=10, 'kaf'=20, 'waw'=6, 'nun'=50, total 86? Actually typical abjad might be slightly different but the text says 'eighty'—possible OCR corruption). The death year is 150 AH (sayf: 'sad'=90, 'ya'=10, 'fa'=80, total 180? Again approximate). The lifespan is 70 (sata: 'sin'=60, 'ta'=9, 'alif'=1, total 70). The other imams are similarly encoded but the specific words are unclear. The purpose is to provide an easy memory aid for important dates.

Key Terms
أحمد بن حنبل
الشافعي
رجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal's life exemplifies piety, and traditional scholars used clever mnemonics like this poem to preserve important historical data.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If you were to devote even half an hour tonight to reading the Quran, would that be a small step toward his example? What is stopping you from starting tonight?
?
Al-Shafi'i declared Imam Ahmad the most knowledgeable, pious, ascetic, and learned person in all of Baghdad. If your closest friend or teacher were asked about you, what would they say? What would you need to change so that they could truthfully praise your piety and knowledge?
?
The scholars encoded valuable knowledge into short poems so it would never be forgotten. If your life were reduced to a few lines for others to remember, what would those lines contain? What knowledge from this lesson will you intentionally memorize so it stays with you beyond this reading?

Life of Imam al-Sawi

Chronology of the Four Imams in a Didactic Poem
Al-Sawi presents a poetic chronology of the Imams' births and deaths using abjad numerals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the abjad numeral system is used in a didactic poem to memorize the birth and death years of the four Sunni imams.

Core Idea
A simple poem encodes the lives of the four great imams using numbers hidden in words.

Al-Sawi, after praising the imams, presents a poetic composition that encodes their birth years, death years, and lifespans using abjad numerals. This clever mnemonic helps students remember important historical data. Let's explore how the poem works and how to decode it.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and its explanation
Read the Arabic poem carefully and the author's breakdown of abjad values. Notice that each imam has three numbers: birth, death, lifespan, each represented by a keyword.
2
Understand the abjad system
Abjad assigns numerical values to Arabic letters. For example, the word 'sayf' (سيف) equals 150. Learn how words correspond to numbers.
This is key to decoding the poem.
3
Apply the calculation
Using the given keywords, verify the birth and death years for each imam. Check that the sum of the numbers matches the lifespan.
For example, Abu Hanifa: birth 80 + lifespan 70 = death 150.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفضل هؤلاء الأئمة أشهر من الشمس في رابعة النهار، ونظم بعضهم تاريخ ولادة الأربعة ووفاتهم ومدة عمرهم بقوله : [من الرجز] تاريخ نعمان يكن سيف سطا ومالك في قطع جوف ضبطا والشَّافعي صين ببر ند وأحمد بسبق أمر جعد فاحسب على ترتيب نظم الشعر ميلادهم فموتهم كالعمر فولادة أبي حنيفة سنة ثمانين وجمله يكن)، ووفاته سنة مئة وخمسين وجمله (سيف)، وعمره سبعون وجمله (سطا). وولادة مالك سنة تسعين، وجمله (في)، ووفاته سنة مئة وتسعة وسبعين، وجمله (قطع)، وعمره تسعة وثمانون، وجمله (جوف). وولادة الشَّافعي سنة مئة وخمسين يوم وفاة أبي حنيفة، وجمله (صين)، ووفاته سنة مئتين وأربع، وجمله (ببر)، وعمره أربع وخمسون، وجمله (ند). وولادة أحمد سنة أربع وستين ومئة وجمله (بسبق)، ووفاته سنة إحدى وأربعين ومئتين وجمله (أمر)، وعمره سبع وسبعون وجمله (جعد) الله وعنا بهم أجمعين.
The virtue of these Imams is more famous than the sun at midday. Some of them composed a poem about the dates of birth and death of the four and the length of their lives. He said (in rajaz meter): The date of Nu'man (Abu Hanifa) is 'sayf' (sword) 'sata' (struck), and Malik is 'qat' (cut) 'jawf' (cavity) captured, and al-Shafi'i is 'sayn' (preserve) 'bibar' (by chill) 'nad' (dew), and Ahmad is 'bisabq' (with preceding) 'amr' (command) 'ja'd' (curl). Then calculate according to the order of the poem's composition: their births, then their deaths, like a lifespan. So the birth of Abu Hanifa was in year 80, and its abjad value is 'yakun' (there is), and his death in year 150, its abjad 'sayf', and his lifespan 70, its abjad 'sata'. The birth of Malik in year 90, its abjad 'fi', and his death in year 179, its abjad 'qat', and his lifespan 89, its abjad 'jawf'. The birth of al-Shafi'i in year 150, the day of Abu Hanifa's death, its abjad 'sayn', and his death in year 204, its abjad 'bibar', and his lifespan 54, its abjad 'nad'. The birth of Ahmad in year 164, its abjad 'bisabq', and his death in year 241, its abjad 'amr', and his lifespan 77, its abjad 'ja'd'. May Allah be pleased with them all.
Source pages p. 446

Commentary
This passage provides a didactic poetic chronology for the four founders of the Sunni legal schools. The author begins by asserting that the virtue of these Imams is more famous than the midday sun. Then he quotes a poem composed by one of the scholars that encodes the birth, death, and lifespan of each Imam using abjad numerals (each letter has a numerical value). The poem gives a keyword for each Imam: for Abu Hanifa (Nu'man) the keywords are 'sayf' and 'sata'; for Malik: 'qat' and 'jawf'; for al-Shafi'i: 'sayn', 'bibar', and 'nad'; for Ahmad: 'bisabq', 'amr', and 'ja'd'. The author then breaks down the abjad values: the birth year of Abu Hanifa is 80 (abjad of 'yakun'), death 150 ('sayf'), lifespan 70 ('sata'). Similarly for Malik: birth 90 ('fi'), death 179 ('qat'), lifespan 89 ('jawf'). For al-Shafi'i: birth 150 ('sayn'), death 204 ('bibar'), lifespan 54 ('nad'). For Ahmad: birth 164 ('bisabq'), death 241 ('amr'), lifespan 77 ('ja'd'). The passage ends with a supplication for divine pleasure upon them all.

أبو حنيفة
مالك
الشافعي
أحمد بن حنبل
حساب الجمل
الرجز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The poem's dates are exact historical records.
Correction: The poem is a mnemonic device; the dates may vary in other sources.

The Life and Teachings of al-Junayd
Al-Sawi gives the biography, teachers, and sayings of the Sufi master Junayd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key biographical details of al-Junayd and his core teachings on tasawwuf.

Core Idea
Al-Junayd: the master of tasawwuf who combined jurisprudence with spiritual practice.

This lesson introduces the revered Sufi master al-Junayd. Al-Sawi provides a concise biography highlighting his lineage, teachers, and two profound sayings that define the essence of Islamic spirituality. Through this passage, we see how early Sufism was firmly grounded in practice and prophetic adherence.

Guided Path
1
Read the biography
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview of al-Junayd's life.
2
Identify the key figures
Note the teachers mentioned and the school of thought (Abu Thawr) he followed.
His uncle al-Sari al-Saqati and al-Harith al-Muhasibi are important early Sufis.
3

Consider the two sayings and what they reveal about the nature of true tasawwuf.
The first saying contrasts idle talk with real practice; the second emphasizes following the Prophet.

أبو القاسم الجنيد ومن تبعه . قوله: (أبو القاسم): هي كنيته واسمه الجنيد محمد، سيد الطائفة الصوفية وإمامهم، نشأ وولد بالعراق، وكان فقيهاً على مذهب أبي ثور، صحب خاله السري السقطي والحارث المحاسبي ومحمد بن علي القصاب، مات سنة سبع وتسعين ومئتين، فهو من أهل القرن الثالث. ومن كلامه : (ما أخذنا التصوف عن القيل والقال، ولكن عن الجوع وترك الدنيا، وقطع المألوفات والمستحسنات). ومن كلامه أيضاً : الطرق كلها مسدودة على الخلق إلا من اقتفى أثر الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام.
Abu al-Qasim al-Junayd and those who followed him. His saying: (Abu al-Qasim): that is his teknonym, and his name is al-Junayd Muhammad, the master of the Sufi group and their imam, he grew up and was born in Iraq, and he was a jurist according to the school of Abu Thawr, he accompanied his maternal uncle al-Sari al-Saqati and al-Harith al-Muhasibi and Muhammad ibn Ali al-Qassab, he died in the year 297, so he is from the third century. Also among his sayings: 'All paths are blocked to creation except for the one who follows the footsteps of the Messenger, peace and blessings be upon him.'

Commentary
This passage presents a biographical entry on Abu al-Qasim al-Junayd, a prominent early Sufi master. The author first gives his kunya (Abu al-Qasim) and name (al-Junayd Muhammad), then describes him as the leader and imam of the Sufi group. He was born and raised in Iraq, and was a jurist following the school of Abu Thawr. His teachers and companions include his maternal uncle al-Sari al-Saqati, al-Harith al-Muhasibi, and Muhammad ibn Ali al-Qassab. His death year is given as 297 AH, placing him in the third century. The passage then quotes two of his notable sayings. The first emphasizes that true tasawwuf is not about idle talk but about spiritual practices: hunger, renunciation of the world, and cutting off attachments to habits and what is deemed good. The second saying stresses that all spiritual paths are closed except for the one who follows the example of the Prophet Muhammad. These sayings highlight the importance of practice and adherence to the Sunna in Sufism.

Abjad Dates of the Three Imams
Al-Sawi lists birth, death, and age of three imams with abjad mnemonic words.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how abjad numerals are used to encode birth and death years in Islamic biographical texts.

Core Idea
Abjad turns dates into memorable words.

Early Muslim scholars often used the abjad system—where each Arabic letter carries a numerical value—to encode important dates. Al-Sawi provides the birth and death years of three major imams, each accompanied by an abjad word that sums to the year. This lesson helps you recognize and appreciate this mnemonic technique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the pattern: for each imam, birth year, death year, and age each have an abjad word in parentheses.
The words look like ordinary Arabic, but they are chosen for their numerical value.
2
Identify the three imams and their data
List the imams: Malik, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad. For each, note the birth year, death year, and age, plus the abjad word. Compare the numbers.
Malik died at 89, al-Shafi'i at 54, Ahmad at 77.
3
Verify the abjad values
Take one abjad word, like (في) for Malik's birth. The letters ف (80) + ي (10) = 90. Confirm it matches the year. Do this for a few more to understand the system.
Abjad values: alif=1, ba=2, jim=3, dal=4, ha=5, waw=6, za=7, ha=8, ta=9, ya=10, etc.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Why use abjad? It aids memorization and adds a layer of spiritual significance. The words themselves may have meanings that reinforce the data.
For example, (قطع) 'qaṭa‘' means 'cut off,' perhaps hinting at death.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وولادة مالك سنة تسعين، وجمله (في)، ووفاته سنة مئة وتسعة وسبعين، وجمله (قطع)، وعمره تسعة وثمانون، وجمله (جوف). وولادة الشَّافعيّ سنة مئة وخمسين يوم وفاة أبي حنيفة، وجمله (صين)، ووفاته سنة مئتين وأربع ، وجمله (ببر) ، وعمره أربع وخمسون، وجمله (ند). وولادة أحمد سنة أربع وستين ومئة وجمله (بسبق)، ووفاته سنة إحدى وأربعين ومئتين وجمله (أمر)، وعمره سبع وسبعون وجمله (جعد ) الله وعنا بهم أجمعين .
And the birth of Malik in the year ninety, and its abjad (fī), and his death in the year one hundred and seventy-nine, and its abjad (qaṭa‘), and his age eighty-nine, and its abjad (jūf). And the birth of al-Shāfi‘ī in the year one hundred and fifty on the day of the death of Abū Ḥanīfa, and its abjad (ṣīn), and his death in the year two hundred and four, and its abjad (bibr), and his age fifty-four, and its abjad (nadd). And the birth of Aḥmad in the year one hundred and sixty-four, and its abjad (bisabq), and his death in the year two hundred and forty-one, and its abjad (amr), and his age seventy-seven, and its abjad (ja‘d). May God be pleased with them all.
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a mnemonic record of the birth and death dates of three major imams: Mālik ibn Anas, Muḥammad ibn Idrīs al-Shāfi‘ī, and Aḥmad ibn Ḥanbal. For each imam, al-Ṣāwī provides the birth year, death year, and age, each accompanied by an abjad phrase in parentheses that encodes the number. Abjad is a system where each Arabic letter has a numerical value; the word given is the sum of its letters. For example, for Mālik, the word 'fī' (فـي) has numerical value 90 (fā=80, yā=10), matching his birth year. The pattern continues: 'qaṭa‘' (قطع) sums to 179 (qāf=100, ṭā=9, ‘ayn=70), 'jūf' (جوف) sums to 89 (jīm=3, wāw=6, fā=80). For al-Shāfi‘ī, 'ṣīn' (صين) is 150 (ṣād=90, yā=10, nūn=50), 'bibr' (ببر) is 204 (bā=2, bā=2, rā=200), 'nadd' (ند) is 54 (nūn=50, dāl=4). For Aḥmad, 'bisabq' (بسبق) is 164 (bā=2, sīn=60, bā=2, qāf=100), 'amr' (أمر) is 241 (alif=1, mīm=40, rā=200), 'ja‘d' (جعد) is 77 (jīm=3, ‘ayn=70, dāl=4). The passage concludes with a prayer for God's pleasure on all of them. This method of encoding dates was common in Islamic biographical literature to aid memorization.

Key Terms
جمله (abjad phrase)
ولادة (birth)
وفاة (death)
عمر (age)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The abjad words are random or meaningless.
Correction: They are carefully chosen: each letter contributes a numerical value that sums to the intended year or age.

Checkpoint
What is the birth year of Imam Malik according to the abjad phrase given?
A) 90 (fī)
B) 179 (qaṭa‘)
C) 89 (jūf)
D) 150 (ṣīn)

Key Takeaway
Abjad numerals turn dates into memorable words, a common mnemonic in Islamic biographical literature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever used a creative method to memorize important dates in your Islamic learning? Could you create an abjad mnemonic for the birth year of a scholar you admire? What would that word be and how would it help you recall the date?

Take a moment to write one such abjad phrase.
?
The abjad method requires care and precision—each letter must be accounted for. How might this attention to detail in preserving knowledge inspire you to be more accurate in your own study of sacred texts? Will you commit to learning the abjad values of the Arabic alphabet this week?

Junayd al-Baghdadi: Life and Foundational Teachings
Biography of Junayd with two sayings and doctrinal commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the life and key teachings of Junayd al-Baghdadi, and why he is considered the imam of the Sufi path.

Core Idea
Junayd combined juristic precision with spiritual practice.

Al-Sawi introduces Junayd as the master of the Sufis, a jurist of the school of Abu Thawr, and a spiritual guide. His sayings emphasize practical renunciation and strict adherence to the Prophet's example, forming the foundation for his role as the leader of the Sufi community.

Guided Path
1
Read the biographical details
Note Junayd's kunya (Abū al-Qāsim), name (al-Junayd Muḥammad), teachers (al-Sarī al-Saqaṭī, al-Ḥārith al-Muḥāsibī, Muḥammad ibn ‘Alī al-Qaṣṣāb), and his fiqh school (Abū Thawr). He was a jurist before a Sufi.
2
Study the first saying
Junayd defines Sufism as practice, not talk: 'We did not take Sufism from idle talk, but from hunger, abandoning the world, and cutting off habits.' What does 'cutting off habits' teach us?
3
Study the second saying
The only open path is following the Prophet: 'All paths are blocked except for the one who follows the trace of the Messenger.' This shows the centrality of the Sunna.
4
Understand the three groups
The text identifies the scholars (theologians/jurists), worshipers (ascetics), and Sufis as the elite of the Muslim community. Salvation requires correct creed according to Ahl al-Sunna, taqlīd of a four-imam madhhab, and then following the path of Junayd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أبو القاسم الجنيد ومن تبعه . قوله: (أبو القاسم): هي هي كنيته واسمه الجنيد محمَّد، سيّد بن الطَّائفة الصُّوفيَّة وإمامهم، نشأ وولد بالعراق، وكان فقيهاً على مذهب أبي ثور، صحب خاله السَّري السقطي والحارث المحاسبي ومحمد بن علي القصاب، مات سنة سبع وتسعين ومئتين، فهو من أهل القرن الثالث. ومن كلامه : (ما أخذنا التَّصوُّف عن القيل والقال، ولكن عن الجوع وترك الدُّنيا، وقطع المألوفات والمستحسنات)(۱). ومن كلامه أيضاً : الطرق كلُّها مسدودة على الخلق إلَّا من اقتفى أثر الرَّسول عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام) (۲). فهؤلاء الفِرَق الثَّلاثة هم خواص الأمة المحمديَّة، ومن عداهم من الفرق على ضلال وإن كان البعض منهم يُحكم له بالإسلام، فالنَّاجي من كان في عقيدته على طبق ما بيَّنه أهل السُّنَّة، وقلَّد في الأحكام العمليَّة إماماً من الأئمة الأربعة المرضيَّة، ثمَّ تمامُ النِّعمة والنَّجاة في سلوك مسلك الجنيد وأتباعه بعد أن أحكم دينه على طبق ما بيَّنه الفريقان المتقدِّمان، وممن سلك مسلكه عنه .... ومن كلامه أيضاً : (لو أقبل صادق على الله ألف ألف سنة ثمَّ أعرض لحظةً ؛ كان ما فاته أكثر ممَّا ناله) (۱) . ومن كلامه أيضاً : (إن بدت ذرّةٌ من عين الكرم والجود؛ ألحقت المسيء المحسن، وبقيت أعمالهم فضلاً لهم . ومن كلامه أيضاً : (من الأعمال ما لا يطلع عليه الحفظة، وهو ذكر الله بالقلب، وما طويت عليه الضَّمائر من الهيبة والتعظيم الله، واعتماد الخوف، وإجلال أوامره ونواهيه)(۲). ومن كلامه أيضاً : احفظوا ساعاتكم؛ فإنَّها زائلة غير راجعة، وصلوا أورادكم تجدوا نفعها في دار الإقامة، ولا يشغلكم عن الله قليل الله قليل الدُّنيا ؛ فإنَّ قليلها يشغل عن كثير الآخرة) (۳) .
Abū al-Qāsim al-Junayd and those who follow him. His saying: (Abū al-Qāsim): it is his kunya and his name is al-Junayd Muḥammad, the master of the Sufi group and their imam, he grew up and was born in Iraq, and he was a jurist according to the school of Abū Thawr, he accompanied his maternal uncle al-Sarī al-Saqaṭī and al-Ḥārith al-Muḥāsibī and Muḥammad ibn ‘Alī al-Qaṣṣāb, he died in the year two hundred and ninety-seven, so he is from the people of the third century. And among his sayings: 'We did not take Sufism from idle talk, but from hunger, abandoning the world, and cutting off habits and desires.' (1) And also among his sayings: 'All paths are blocked to creation except for the one who follows the trace of the Messenger, peace and blessings be upon him.' (2) These three groups are the elite of the Muḥammadan community, and whoever is other than them among the groups is in misguidance, even if some of them are judged to be Muslim. The saved one is he whose creed is in accordance with what the People of the Sunna have clarified, and who follows an imam from the four approved imams in practical rulings, then the completion of grace and salvation is in following the path of al-Junayd and his followers after he has perfected his religion according to what the two preceding groups clarified. And among those who followed his path... And also among his sayings: 'If a truthful person turns to God for a thousand thousand years and then turns away for a moment, what he missed is greater than what he attained.' (1) And also among his sayings: 'If an atom of generosity and munificence appears from the eye, it joins the evildoer to the good-doer, and their deeds remain as a favor to them.' And also among his sayings: 'There are deeds that the recording angels do not see — that is, the remembrance of God in the heart, and what the consciences contain of awe and veneration of God, and reliance on fear, and reverence for His commands and prohibitions.' (2) And also among his sayings: 'Preserve your hours, for they are fleeting and do not return, and perform your litanies (awrād) and you will find their benefit in the abode of permanence, and let not the small of this world occupy you from God, for its small occupies from the much of the Hereafter.' (3)
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry on Abū al-Qāsim al-Junayd al-Baghdādī, one of the most prominent early Sufi masters, followed by a collection of his sayings. The entry first clarifies his kunya and name: Abū al-Qāsim is his kunya, and his given name is al-Junayd Muḥammad. He is described as the master (sayyid) and imam of the Sufi community. He was born and raised in Iraq, and was a jurist following the school of Abū Thawr (a now-extinct madhhab). He studied under his maternal uncle al-Sarī al-Saqaṭī, as well as al-Ḥārith al-Muḥāsibī and Muḥammad ibn ‘Alī al-Qaṣṣāb, all renowned early Sufis. He died in 297 AH, placing him in the third Islamic century. Then his sayings are quoted. The first saying emphasizes that Sufism is not learned from talk but from practice: hunger, renunciation, and cutting attachments. The second saying stresses that all spiritual paths are blocked except for those who follow the Prophet's example. The text then digresses to comment on 'these three groups' — likely referring to scholars, worshipers, and Sufis (the three groups mentioned earlier in the book). It states that these three are the elite of the Muslim community and that salvation lies in correct creed according to Ahl al-Sunna, following one of the four imams in practice, and then following the path of al-Junayd for spiritual purification. This shows the integration of outward (fiqh) and inward (tasawwuf) dimensions. After this digression, more sayings of Junayd are given. The fourth saying warns that even a moment of turning away from God outweighs millennia of devotion. The fifth saying speaks of God's overwhelming mercy that can elevate sinners to the level of the righteous. The sixth saying describes hidden deeds of the heart that angels do not record, such as inward remembrance, awe, and reverence. The final saying exhorts one to guard one's time, perform regular litanies (awrād), and prioritize the Hereafter over the distractions of this world.

Key Terms
الجنيد
التصوف
القيل والقال
إقتفاء الأثر
الخواص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Junayd rejected all formal religious knowledge because he emphasized practice over talk.
Correction: Junayd was himself a jurist (faqīh) and studied under scholars; his rejection of 'idle talk' means prioritizing practice, not abandoning knowledge.

Checkpoint

A) Hunger and poverty
B) Following the Messenger
C) Isolation from people
D) Reciting litanies

Key Takeaway
Junayd al-Baghdadi synthesized outward adherence to Sharia with inward purification, and his path is considered the completion of salvation for the elite.

?

Junayd's Sayings on Divine Mercy and Inward Worship
Sayings of Junayd on mercy, hidden worship, and time management.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Junayd's teachings on divine mercy, hidden worship, and the fleeting nature of time.

Core Idea
Even an atom of God's mercy can save, and true worship is hidden in the heart.

Junayd’s later sayings delve into God’s overwhelming mercy, the secrets of worship that angels do not record, and the urgency of preserving one’s moments. They encourage hope and vigilance, complementing his earlier emphasis on practice.

Guided Path
1
Read the saying on divine mercy
Junayd says that if an atom of divine generosity appears, it can join the evildoer to the good-doer, leaving their deeds as a favor to them. Reflect on the vastness of God's mercy.
2
Read the saying on hidden deeds
Some deeds are not seen by the recording angels: remembrance of God in the heart, and inward awe, fear, and reverence. Consider the importance of inner states.
3
Read the exhortation on time
Junayd urges preserving hours, performing litanies (awrād), and not letting the small things of this world distract from the Hereafter. What does this tell us about time management?
4
Connect the sayings
These sayings together show that while God's mercy is vast, the seeker must still be vigilant in worship, both outward and inward.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أبو القاسم الجنيد ومن تبعه . قوله: (أبو القاسم): هي هي كنيته واسمه الجنيد محمَّد، سيّد بن الطَّائفة الصُّوفيَّة وإمامهم، نشأ وولد بالعراق، وكان فقيهاً على مذهب أبي ثور، صحب خاله السَّري السقطي والحارث المحاسبي ومحمد بن علي القصاب، مات سنة سبع وتسعين ومئتين، فهو من أهل القرن الثالث. ومن كلامه : (ما أخذنا التَّصوُّف عن القيل والقال، ولكن عن الجوع وترك الدُّنيا، وقطع المألوفات والمستحسنات)(۱). ومن كلامه أيضاً : الطرق كلُّها مسدودة على الخلق إلَّا من اقتفى أثر الرَّسول عليه الصَّلاة والسَّلام) (۲). فهؤلاء الفِرَق الثَّلاثة هم خواص الأمة المحمديَّة، ومن عداهم من الفرق على ضلال وإن كان البعض منهم يُحكم له بالإسلام، فالنَّاجي من كان في عقيدته على طبق ما بيَّنه أهل السُّنَّة، وقلَّد في الأحكام العمليَّة إماماً من الأئمة الأربعة المرضيَّة، ثمَّ تمامُ النِّعمة والنَّجاة في سلوك مسلك الجنيد وأتباعه بعد أن أحكم دينه على طبق ما بيَّنه الفريقان المتقدِّمان، وممن سلك مسلكه عنه .... ومن كلامه أيضاً : (لو أقبل صادق على الله ألف ألف سنة ثمَّ أعرض لحظةً ؛ كان ما فاته أكثر ممَّا ناله) (۱) . ومن كلامه أيضاً : (إن بدت ذرّةٌ من عين الكرم والجود؛ ألحقت المسيء المحسن، وبقيت أعمالهم فضلاً لهم . ومن كلامه أيضاً : (من الأعمال ما لا يطلع عليه الحفظة، وهو ذكر الله بالقلب، وما طويت عليه الضَّمائر من الهيبة والتعظيم الله، واعتماد الخوف، وإجلال أوامره ونواهيه)(۲). ومن كلامه أيضاً : احفظوا ساعاتكم؛ فإنَّها زائلة غير راجعة، وصلوا أورادكم تجدوا نفعها في دار الإقامة، ولا يشغلكم عن الله قليل الله قليل الدُّنيا ؛ فإنَّ قليلها يشغل عن كثير الآخرة) (۳) .
Abū al-Qāsim al-Junayd and those who follow him. His saying: (Abū al-Qāsim): it is his kunya and his name is al-Junayd Muḥammad, the master of the Sufi group and their imam, he grew up and was born in Iraq, and he was a jurist according to the school of Abū Thawr, he accompanied his maternal uncle al-Sarī al-Saqaṭī and al-Ḥārith al-Muḥāsibī and Muḥammad ibn ‘Alī al-Qaṣṣāb, he died in the year two hundred and ninety-seven, so he is from the people of the third century. And among his sayings: 'We did not take Sufism from idle talk, but from hunger, abandoning the world, and cutting off habits and desires.' (1) And also among his sayings: 'All paths are blocked to creation except for the one who follows the trace of the Messenger, peace and blessings be upon him.' (2) These three groups are the elite of the Muḥammadan community, and whoever is other than them among the groups is in misguidance, even if some of them are judged to be Muslim. The saved one is he whose creed is in accordance with what the People of the Sunna have clarified, and who follows an imam from the four approved imams in practical rulings, then the completion of grace and salvation is in following the path of al-Junayd and his followers after he has perfected his religion according to what the two preceding groups clarified. And among those who followed his path... And also among his sayings: 'If a truthful person turns to God for a thousand thousand years and then turns away for a moment, what he missed is greater than what he attained.' (1) And also among his sayings: 'If an atom of generosity and munificence appears from the eye, it joins the evildoer to the good-doer, and their deeds remain as a favor to them.' And also among his sayings: 'There are deeds that the recording angels do not see — that is, the remembrance of God in the heart, and what the consciences contain of awe and veneration of God, and reliance on fear, and reverence for His commands and prohibitions.' (2) And also among his sayings: 'Preserve your hours, for they are fleeting and do not return, and perform your litanies (awrād) and you will find their benefit in the abode of permanence, and let not the small of this world occupy you from God, for its small occupies from the much of the Hereafter.' (3)
Source pages p. 447

Commentary
This passage is a biographical entry on Abū al-Qāsim al-Junayd al-Baghdādī, one of the most prominent early Sufi masters, followed by a collection of his sayings. The entry first clarifies his kunya and name: Abū al-Qāsim is his kunya, and his given name is al-Junayd Muḥammad. He is described as the master (sayyid) and imam of the Sufi community. He was born and raised in Iraq, and was a jurist following the school of Abū Thawr (a now-extinct madhhab). He studied under his maternal uncle al-Sarī al-Saqaṭī, as well as al-Ḥārith al-Muḥāsibī and Muḥammad ibn ‘Alī al-Qaṣṣāb, all renowned early Sufis. He died in 297 AH, placing him in the third Islamic century. Then his sayings are quoted. The first saying emphasizes that Sufism is not learned from talk but from practice: hunger, renunciation, and cutting attachments. The second saying stresses that all spiritual paths are blocked except for those who follow the Prophet's example. The text then digresses to comment on 'these three groups' — likely referring to scholars, worshipers, and Sufis (the three groups mentioned earlier in the book). It states that these three are the elite of the Muslim community and that salvation lies in correct creed according to Ahl al-Sunna, following one of the four imams in practice, and then following the path of al-Junayd for spiritual purification. This shows the integration of outward (fiqh) and inward (tasawwuf) dimensions. After this digression, more sayings of Junayd are given. The fourth saying warns that even a moment of turning away from God outweighs millennia of devotion. The fifth saying speaks of God's overwhelming mercy that can elevate sinners to the level of the righteous. The sixth saying describes hidden deeds of the heart that angels do not record, such as inward remembrance, awe, and reverence. The final saying exhorts one to guard one's time, perform regular litanies (awrād), and prioritize the Hereafter over the distractions of this world.

Key Terms
الأوراد
الحفظة
الضَّمائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Junayd, which deeds are not seen by the recording angels?
A) Public charity
B) Ritual prayers
C) Remembrance of God in the heart
D) Fasting

Key Takeaway
Junayd teaches that while God's mercy is boundless, the seeker must still guard their time, cultivate inner devotion, and perform regular worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on your past good deeds, assuming they guarantee your future? If a single moment of heedlessness can outweigh a thousand years of devotion, what daily habit must you guard most fiercely?
?
When you sin, do you despair of God's mercy, or do you presume upon it? Which saying of Junayd calls you to a middle path, and how will you respond in your next moment of failure?
?
Consider your last hour of distraction—scrolling, worrying, or idleness. Junayd commands: 'Preserve your hours.' What one small distraction will you cut today to reclaim time for the Hereafter? Be specific.

The Worship and Spiritual Discipline of Ahmad al-Rifai
Description of al-Rifai's daily worship and fasting practice

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the extraordinary devotional practices of Ahmad al-Rifai and his principle that helping fellow believers is as virtuous as fasting.

Core Idea
Intense worship combined with selfless hospitality defines al-Rifai's path.

This passage introduces us to Ahmad al-Rifai, a towering figure in Islamic spirituality. It highlights his rigorous daily worship—four hundred rak'ahs and perpetual fasting—and his beautiful principle that breaking fast to serve brethren is not less in virtue than fasting itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation describing al-Rifai's litanies and fasting practice. Notice the specific numbers and his statement about helping brothers.
2
Identify the key practices
Identify the two main practices: four hundred rak'ahs daily and perpetual fasting. Observe the exception: he breaks fast when brothers visit.
3
Understand the principle
Reflect on al-Rifai's statement: assisting brethren is not less than the virtue of fasting. This shows the balance between worship and community service.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القطب الرَّبانيُّ الإمام سيّدي أحمد ابن الرفاعي وأتباعه، وكان من أوراده أربع مئة ركعة كلَّ يوم، وكان صائم الدهر لا يفطر إلَّا إذا دخل عليه إخوانه فيأكل معهم وهو ساكت ويقول : (ليست المساعدة مع الإخوان بأقل من فضل الصّوم) (۱). لحظةً دخل عليه إبليس في صورة نقيب فقال : أريد أن أخدمك بلا أجرة، فقال له : (افعل) فأقام يخدمه عشر سنين فلم يجد قلبه غافلاً عن ربه واحدة، فطلب الانصراف وقال له : أنا إبليس، فقال له: (عرفتك أوّل ما دخلت، وإِنَّما استخدمتك عقوبة لك ؛ فإنَّه لا ثواب لأعمالك الآن في خرة فقال : ما رأيت قوَّتك يا جنيد فقال : اذهب يا ملعون أتريد أن تدخل عليَّ الإعجاب بنفسي؟!)، ثم خرج خاسئاً (۲)، خاسئاً(٢)، وفضله كالشَّمس في رابعة النَّهار، ألحقنا الله بنسبه، وحققنا بحسبه
The spiritual pole, the imam, my master Ahmad ibn al-Rifai and his followers. Among his litanies were four hundred rak'ahs every day, and he was perpetually fasting, breaking his fast only when his brethren came to him, so he would eat with them in silence and say: 'Assisting the brethren is not less than the virtue of fasting.' (1) A moment [later?], Iblis entered upon him in the form of a deputy and said: 'I want to serve you without wages.' He said to him: 'Do so.' So he stayed serving him for ten years and did not find his heart heedless of his Lord for a single [moment]. So he requested to leave and said to him: 'I am Iblis.' He said to him: 'I recognized you from the first time you entered. I only employed you as a punishment for you, for there is no reward for your works now in the Hereafter.' So he said: 'I have never seen your strength, O Junayd.' He said: 'Go away, O accursed one. Do you want to make me admire myself?!' Then he left disgraced. (2) And his virtue is like the sun at midday. May God join us to his lineage and realize us according to his standing.
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage provides a brief biography and spiritual anecdotes about Ahmad al-Rifai. It first describes him as 'the spiritual pole' (al-qutb al-rabbani) and notes his intense worship: he recited four hundred rak'ahs daily and observed perpetual fasting, only breaking his fast when visitors came, eating with them in silence while stating that helping brethren is not inferior to the virtue of fasting. Then a story follows: Iblis appeared to him disguised as a deputy offering free service. Al-Rifai accepted, and Iblis served for ten years without once finding al-Rifai's heart forgetful of God. When Iblis revealed his identity, al-Rifai said he knew from the start and that he employed Iblis as a punishment (because Iblis's acts now earn no reward). Iblis then addressed him as 'O Junayd' (possibly a slip or address to Junayd, but the narrative continues about al-Rifai's virtue). The story concludes with Iblis leaving disgraced. The passage ends praising al-Rifai's virtue as brilliant as the midday sun, with a prayer that God join the reader to his lineage and grant them his spiritual station.

Key Terms
القطب الرَّبانيُّ
أوراد
صائم الدهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that breaking a perpetual fast for visitors is a lapse in piety.
Correction: Al-Rifai explicitly states that assisting the brethren is not less than the virtue of fasting, showing that serving others is equally meritorious.

Checkpoint
What did al-Rifai say about helping his brethren?
A) It is less than fasting
B) It is not less than the virtue of fasting
C) It cancels the fasting
D) It is required only during Ramadan

Key Takeaway
Al-Rifai's intense worship was balanced by a deep commitment to serving his community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you are in the middle of a personal act of worship like fasting or prayer, and a brother or sister in need comes to you, do you prioritize your devotion or their need? What does al-Rifai's example compel you to reconsider?
?
Do you secretly believe that private worship is more spiritual than serving others? How does the statement 'Assisting the brethren is not less than the virtue of fasting' challenge your assumptions about what pleases Allah most?
?
Al-Rifai ate with his brethren in silence. Do you allow your acts of service to be tainted by a desire for conversation, recognition, or reward? Would you be willing to serve quietly, expecting nothing from people, knowing you are equal to the one who fasts perpetually?

The Encounter with Iblis: A Lesson in Spiritual Vigilance
Anecdote of Iblis serving al-Rifai and the spiritual response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the story of Ahmad al-Rifai's encounter with Iblis and the spiritual lessons it teaches about constant awareness of God and humility.

Core Idea
Unwavering mindfulness of God defeats hidden spiritual traps.

This remarkable story recounts how Iblis disguised himself to serve Ahmad al-Rifai for ten years, hoping to find a moment of heedlessness. Al-Rifai's unbroken awareness of God and his humble dismissal of Iblis provide profound lessons in spiritual vigilance and protection from self-admiration.

Guided Path
1
Read the story
Read the Arabic and translation of the narrative. Follow the sequence: Iblis appears, serves ten years, reveals himself, al-Rifai responds, and Iblis leaves.
2
Analyze al-Rifai's response
Notice that al-Rifai recognized Iblis immediately but allowed him to serve as a punishment. Reflect on why this is a punishment.
Iblis's actions now earn no reward in the Hereafter.
3
Draw the lesson on humility
When Iblis tries to praise him ('I have never seen your strength'), al-Rifai rebukes him, fearing self-admiration. This shows the importance of guarding against pride.

Early Life of Ahmad al-Rifai
Al-Manawi provides lineage, birth, and early education of Ahmad al-Rifai.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the early life and education of Ahmad al-Rifai as recorded by al-Manawi, including his lineage, birth, and training in Shafi'i jurisprudence and Sufism.

Core Idea
Even great Sufi saints were grounded in classical Islamic scholarship.

This lesson introduces a biographical note from al-Manawi's work on the early life of Ahmad al-Rifai, a towering figure in Sufism. The passage traces his lineage, his birthplace and year, and his dual training in Shafi'i jurisprudence and Sufi practice. It reminds us that the great saints of Islam were often products of rigorous traditional learning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Let the biographical details sink in.
The Arabic ends mid-sentence; it continues in the source.
2
Identify key biographical elements
Note the full lineage, the kunya and nisba, the year of birth (500 AH), the place (Umm Ubayda), and his education in Shafi'i fiqh and tasawwuf.
3
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that al-Rifai was purely a Sufi without formal training. This passage shows he studied Shafi'i jurisprudence seriously.
The author uses the word 'tafaqqaha' – he became a jurist.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (سيدي أحمد ابن الرِّفاعي) : قال المناوي في «الكواكب الدُّرِّيَّة في مناقب الصُّوفيَّة : (هو أحمد بن علي بن أحمد بن يحيى بن حازم بن رفاعة ، [الشيخ] الزَّاهد الكبير، أحد الأولياء المشاهير، أبو العباس الرفاعي المغربي ، [ كان سيّداً جليلاً] صوفيّاً عظيماً نبيلاً . قدم أبوه من العراق وسكن أم عبيدة بأرض البطائح، وولد بها سنة خمس مئة، ونشأ بها وتفقه على مذهب الشافعي وتصوّف، وجاهد نفسه
His saying: 'My master Ahmad ibn al-Rifai': al-Manawi said in 'al-Kawakib al-Durriyya fi Manaqib al-Sufiyya': 'He is Ahmad ibn Ali ibn Ahmad ibn Yahya ibn Hazim ibn Rifa'a, the great ascetic sheikh, one of the famous saints, Abu al-Abbas al-Rifai al-Maghribi, he was a noble master, a great noble Sufi. His father came from Iraq and settled in Umm Ubayda in the land of al-Bata'ih, and he was born there in the year five hundred, and grew up there, and studied jurisprudence according to the Shafi'i school, and became a Sufi, and strove against himself.'
Source pages p. 449

Commentary
This passage is a marginal commentary (qawluhu) that glosses the name 'my master Ahmad ibn al-Rifai' by quoting al-Manawi's work 'al-Kawakib al-Durriyya'. It gives a formal biography: his full lineage tracing back to his ancestor Rifa'a; he is described as a great ascetic and famous saint, with the kunya Abu al-Abbas and the nisba al-Rifai al-Maghribi (though his origin is Iraqi). His father emigrated from Iraq to Umm Ubayda in the marshlands (al-Bata'ih), where al-Rifai was born in the year 500 AH. He grew up there, studied jurisprudence (fiqh) following the Shafi'i school, and engaged in Sufism (tasawwuf), and he struggled against his own soul (jahada nafschi). The passage ends abruptly, likely continuing on the next page.

Key Terms
الشافعي
التصوّف
مذهب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Rifai was only a Sufi saint without formal religious education.
Correction: The passage explicitly states that he studied jurisprudence (tafaqqaha) according to the Shafi'i school, showing he had a strong foundation in Islamic law.

Checkpoint
According to al-Manawi, what madhhab did Ahmad al-Rifai follow?
A) The Hanbali madhhab.
B) The Maliki madhhab.
C) The Shafi'i madhhab.
D) He did not follow any madhhab.

Key Takeaway
Al-Rifai was both a Shafi'i jurist and a Sufi saint, combining formal scholarship with asceticism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Rifai combined formal fiqh study with Sufism and self-struggle. In your own life, do you give equal weight to knowledge and spiritual practice, or do you neglect one? What specific change will you make this week to restore balance?
?
The passage ends with 'he strove against himself' – a continuous action. When was the last time you truly struggled against your own desires for the sake of Allah? If it has been a while, what is holding you back from starting today?
?
You were given a specific time, place, and upbringing just like al-Rifai. Are you using your circumstances to build both knowledge and character? Be honest: what is one area where you are avoiding the hard work of self-discipline?

The Miracles of Ahmad al-Rifa'i
Narrates two miracles of Ahmad al-Rifa'i: the Prophet's hand from the grave and a garden-for-paradise transaction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two remarkable miracles attributed to the Sufi saint Ahmad al-Rifa'i and how they illustrate his spiritual station.

Core Idea
Miracles confirm the saint's closeness to Allah and the Prophet.

This passage from al-Sawi's commentary recounts two extraordinary events from the life of Ahmad al-Rifa'i, a revered Sufi saint. The first involves a physical manifestation from the Prophet's grave, and the second a heavenly transaction that is divinely validated. As you read, notice how these stories function as hagiographic proof of his sanctity.

Guided Path
1
Read the miracle narratives
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the sequence of events: the hand from the grave and the garden purchase.
These are not doctrinal claims but hagiographic accounts.
2
Analyze the hand-from-the-grave miracle
Consider how the Prophet's hand emerging is presented as a reward for Ahmad's devotion and a public spectacle.
It emphasizes the living relationship between the saint and the Prophet.
3
Study the garden transaction
Notice the seller's demand for a palace in Paradise, Ahmad's written guarantee, and the divine inscription on the grave.
This illustrates the saint's confidence in Allah's promise.
4
Reflect on the purpose
Ask yourself: why are these stories included in a theological commentary? They serve to inspire and affirm the possibility of miracles for awliya.
They are meant to strengthen faith, not to establish dogma.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لمَّا حجَّ وقف تجاه الحجرة الشريفة النبوية وأنشد: كنتُ أُرسلها تقبل الأرض عنِّي فهي نائبتي في حالة البعد روحي وهذه نوبة الأشباح قد حضرت فامدد يمينك كي تحظى بها شفتي فخرجت اليد الشَّريفة من القبر حتّى قبّلها والنَّاس ينظرون إليها. وأخبر بوقت موته وصفته، فكان كما قال. وأراد شراء بستان، فأبى صاحبه ألا يبيعه إلَّا بقصر في الجنَّة، فارتعد وتغيَّر واصفر، ثمَّ قال: قد اشتريت منك بذلك، قال: اكتب لي خطّك، فكتب: بسم الله الرّحمن الرّحيم، هذا ما ابتاع إسـمـاعـيــل مـن العبد أحمد ابن الرفاعي ضامناً على كرم الله له قصراً في الجنَّة، يحُدُّه حدود؛ الأوَّل: لجنَّة عدن الثَّاني: لجنَّة المأوى، الثالث: لجنة الخلد، الرابع: لجنة الفردوس بجميع حوره وولدان وفرشه وأشربته وأنهاره وأشجاره عوضاً عن بستانه في الدُّنيا، والله شاهد على ذلك وكفيل، مات إسماعيل؛ دفنت معه الورقة فأصبحوا وإذا مكتوب على قبره: قد وجدنا ما وعدنا ربُّنا حقاً. فلمَّا مات ببلده سنة ثمان وسبعين وخمس مئة، ولم يعقب.
When he performed the pilgrimage, he stood facing the noble prophetic chamber and recited: 'I used to send her (my soul) to kiss the earth for me, so she is my deputy in the state of distance, and now this is the turn of the bodies, so I have come; extend your hand so that my lips may obtain it.' Then the noble hand came out of the grave until he kissed it, and the people were looking at it. And he informed (others) of the time and description of his death, and it was as he said. And he wanted to buy a garden, but its owner refused to sell it except for a palace in Paradise. So he trembled, changed color, and turned pale. Then he said: 'I have bought it from you for that.' The owner said: 'Write me your deed.' So he wrote: 'In the name of Allah, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful. This is what Isma'il purchased from the servant Ahmad ibn al-Rifa'i, guaranteeing upon Allah's generosity a palace for him in Paradise, bounded by four boundaries: first, the Garden of Eden; second, the Garden of Refuge; third, the Garden of Eternity; fourth, the Garden of Paradise, with all its houris, boys, furnishings, drinks, rivers, and trees, in exchange for his garden in this world. And Allah is witness and guarantor.' Isma'il died; the paper was buried with him, and in the morning they found written on his grave: 'We have found true what our Lord promised us.' Then he died in his town in the year 578 AH, and left no offspring.
Source pages p. 451

Commentary
This passage narrates several miracles (karamat) associated with the Sufi saint Ahmad al-Rifa'i. The first miracle: while performing the Hajj, he recites a poem expressing his longing for the Prophet, and the Prophet's hand emerges from the grave to be kissed, witnessed by many people. This is a classic hagiographic motif affirming the saint's closeness to the Prophet. Second, he accurately predicts his own death. Third, a remarkable transaction: a man refuses to sell a garden except for a palace in Paradise. Ahmad al-Rifa'i accepts, writing a deed guaranteeing the palace on Allah's behalf, listing the four levels of Paradise as the boundaries. After the buyer dies, the deed is buried with him, and a divine inscription appears on the grave confirming the fulfillment of the promise. The passage concludes with the saint's death in 578 AH without heirs, emphasizing his detachment from worldly succession.

يد شريفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the Prophet's physical hand literally emerged from the grave as a regular occurrence or that this is a core Islamic doctrine.
Correction: These are hagiographic accounts meant to inspire, not dogmatic claims. Miracles of saints (karamat) are accepted in Sunni Islam but are not part of essential belief.

A) A sum of gold coins

D) A piece of land in exchange

Key Takeaway
Ahmad al-Rifa'i's miracles, including the Prophet's hand from the grave and the heavenly transaction, highlight the special graces Allah grants to His awliya.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When Ahmad recited his poem longing for the Prophet, his soul had kissed the earth from afar, but his body yearned to touch the Prophet's hand. Do you have that same burning desire to be near the Prophet? What is holding you back from nurturing that longing in your daily life? Commit to one act today that increases your love for him.
?
The seller asked for a palace in Paradise, and Ahmad trembled and turned pale. If you were faced with a transaction that required risking your worldly security for the unseen, would you hesitate? Examine your heart: are you willing to trade something you hold dear for the promise of Paradise, or do you cling to this world? Write down one worldly attachment you will surrender to Allah today.
?
The deed was written with Allah as witness and guarantor, and it was honored. Do you live your life as if every promise you make is witnessed by Allah? Is there a covenant you have broken or neglected? Repent and renew your commitment to that promise now.
